Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
ii
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
Environmental concerns are driving the need for sustainable construction methods. This study
investigates dismantling techniques for precast concrete element buildings in Denmark, emphasiz­
ing Design for Disassembly (DfD). It explores the use of weak mortar joints and hydrodemolition
as tools to enable the reuse of building components, reduce waste, and minimize CO2 emissions.
Through a case study and Finite Element modeling (FEM), insights are provided into the mechan­
ical performance of weak mortar joints under various load conditions. The research also compares
hydrodemolition with traditional methods like mechanical and diamond cutting in terms of effi­
ciency, cost, and environmental impact.
The key findings highlight the potential of hydrodemolition, combined with weak mortar, to enable
safe and efficient disassembly while preserving structural elements for reuse. The study also em­
phasizes the importance of integrating digital tools and circular construction practices to promote
sustainability in the construction industry.
Keywords: Design for Disassembly (DfD), Precast Concrete Elements, Hydrodemolition, Weak
Mortar Joints, Circular Construction, Demolition Methods.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark

Preface
This master’s thesis was prepared as part of the Master of Science in Civil Engineering program
at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU), under the Department of Civil and Mechanical
Engineering. The thesis was conducted between September 2024 and January 2025.
The project, titled Design of Dismantling Methods for Precast Concrete Element Buildings in Den­
mark, represents 30 ECTS credits. The work was supervised by Associate Professor Philip Skov
Halding, within the Department of Civil and Mechanical Engineering at DTU.
Acknowledgments
We would like to express our deepest gratitude to our supervisor, Philip Skov Halding, for his
guidance, support, and insightful feedback throughout this process. Your expertise and enthusiasm
for concrete structures and Design for Disassembly (DfD) has been a constant source of inspiration,
and the knowledge you shared has significantly enhanced our project.
We would like to thank Rambøll for providing the essential data for our case study.
We would like to thank Giuseppe Sciacca for all his help with Robot Structural Analysis and related
calculations. Your help with the project has been of high value for us.
A special acknowledgment goes to Conjet for taking the time to participate in interviews and an­
swer our many questions. Your insights have been highly valuable for our project. Additionally,
we extend our gratitude to everyone who contributed by responding to our email inquiries and
providing thoughtful and helpful answers.
Finally, we would like to thank our partners, families and friends who have supported us during
this project and through our whole studies.

==PAGE:10==
1
Introduction
For over the past 11,700 years, the planet has been in a period of environmental stability called
the Holocene and is now entering a period known as the Anthropocene, where human activities
are starting to push the Earth beyond its ’planetary boundaries’ by having a profound influence on
Earth’s ecological and climate systems [1]. These planetary boundaries define the safe operating
space for humanity. With several of these boundaries already overstepped, the need to take action is
more urgent than ever. If nothing is done, more boundaries may be crossed, leading to irreversible
environmental damage [2]. The building and construction sector is one of the major contributors to
global CO2 emissions, accounting for around 40% of the total emissions when including building
operations and material production [3]. Concrete remains a dominant material in construction, with
cement production alone contributing approximately 8% of global carbon emissions, as highlighted
in the Chatham House report on low­carbon cement and concrete [4].
Concrete’s widespread use is supported by a strong supply chain, despite the availability of more
sustainable alternatives. According to the United Nations Environment Programme [5] over the
past 65 years, concrete consumption has increased tenfold, surpassing steel and timber. Currently
the concrete being produced contains less than 1% of recycled materials. Promoting modular pre­
cast concrete designed for reuse can significantly reduce waste and emissions. Up to 25% of
emissions can be cut by updating building codes and training professionals in de­carbonization
methods. Key strategies include lowering the clinker content in cement, using renewable energy,
and adopting carbon capture. Prefabricating modular units for easy disassembly and reuse is vital
for circular construction and reducing demolition waste [5].
1.1
Motivation of study
The need for sustainable construction drives the exploration of methods to reuse structural el­
ements. Current dismantling techniques often damage precast concrete, restricting re­usability.
This study focuses on Design for Disassembly (DfD) using weak mortar joints and hydrodemo­
lition to find efficient methods that preserve element integrity, minimize waste, and improve the
reuse of precast components.
Buildings today are designed with one purpose, without considering if the purpose of the structure
needs to change, such as converting from residential to office space. In such cases, demolition and
rebuilding is required, but if elements could be swapped without damaging everything, that would
be ideal. For instance, using weaker mortar in conventional precast building allows for the mortar
to be sprayed off and then the slabs for example can be replaced with thicker slabs due to increased
loads or the existing slabs could be reused in a new building.
1.2
Research questions
How can the use of weak mortar joints and hydrodemolition improve the disassembly and reuse of
precast concrete elements in sustainable construction?
What are the benefits and limitations of using weak mortar in joint connections to facilitate the
disassembly of precast concrete elements?
What measures are currently being taken, or could be implemented, to facilitate and encourage cir­
cular construction and the reuse of elements, and how can they support the adoption of dismantling
methods for element reuse?
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
1
==PAGE:11==
1.2.1
Research methodology
• Study of available literature
• Opinion of experts, interviews
• FEM modeling
• Calculations of case study
• Cost and CO2 estimation
1.3
Structure of the report
1.4
Objectives of the thesis
To explore and evaluate methods for dismantling precast concrete elements in buildings DfD, with
a focus on hydrodemolition and weak mortar joints. To compare hydrodemolition with traditional
mechanical methods in terms of safety, efficiency, and preservation of elements for reuse.
To analyze the economic and environmental benefits of implementing advanced dismantling tech­
niques in the context of circular construction.
1.5
Scope and limitations
Scope
• The study focuses on the reuse of precast concrete elements
• It includes studying weak mortar joints to make disassembly easier without damaging the
elements.
• Hydrodemolition technology is examined as a potential dismantling tool, comparing cost,
CO2 emissions and its effectiveness to traditional methods.
• The study involves modeling and testing these methods within the context of a typical precast
concrete building, using finite element analysis (FEM) and practical case studies.
• Circular Construction, explores how circular construction practices can enable the reuse of
structural elements and support dismantling methods.
Limitations
• Limitations in literature review due to lack of available data
• While theoretical evaluations and case studies are included the research may lack extensive
experimental testing of dismantling methods due to time and resource constraints.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
2
==PAGE:12==
2
Literature review
2.1
Design for disassembly and building life cycle
DfD is the principle of planning for disassembly in the design phase ensuring that valuable build­
ing components, elements and materials can be systematically taken apart and reused or reconfig­
ured while remaining in good condition after the disassembly process. The current conventional
methods of designing and constructing concrete buildings do not support this objective making
disassembly hard and costly leading to total demolition the only option in most cases [6].
Figure 2.1: Illustration of building layers and their expected lifetime [6]
In the book Cradle­to­Cradle, the current industrial infrastructure is described as a linear system
focused on delivering products to consumers quickly and cheaply, with little consideration beyond
production. This approach follows a ”cradle­to­grave” model, where products are discarded after
use, prompting customers to buy new ones [7]. This consumption­driven model continues to dom­
inate many industries today. It is estimated that over 90% of materials extracted for durable goods
in the United States become waste almost immediately after production [8].
Figure 2.2: Cradle to grave model [9]
The ideal material is one that can be reused multiple times while maintaining acceptable quality,
recycled (as in McDonough’s cradle­to­cradle model), burned for energy recovery, or decomposed
naturally, all with minimal to no harmful emissions [7].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
3
==PAGE:13==
Figure 2.3: Cradle to cradle model [9]
There is still a lack of standards and guidelines for DfD, but progress is being made every year. For
instance Denmark has published a handbook on DfD, which provides recommendations for reduc­
ing construction waste by designing buildings with DfD in mind. The Danish handbook provides
practical guidelines for disassembling elements at different levels, making sure that components
can be reused and or recycled in an efficient way. The handbook also emphasizes the importance
of industrialized modular design, use of mechanical connections and non­toxic materials that make
sure for an easy separation without damaging the elements [10].
ISO 20887:2020 [11] primarily provides general guidelines for DfD and adaptability in buildings
rather than specific, detailed guidelines for individual components like connections. The standard
emphasizes overall strategies and principles that promote easier disassembly and adaptability, fo­
cusing on:
Modularity: Designing buildings with modular components that can be easily removed, replaced,
or reconfigured.
Material recovery: Prioritizing materials and components that can be reused or recycled at the
end of a building’s life.
Minimizing waste: Strategies to reduce waste during deconstruction or refurbishment.
Life cycle thinking: Considering how buildings can adapt to changes in use over time or be dis­
mantled efficiently.
Connections are discussed in the context of these principles. However, it encourages the use of
mechanical connections (like bolts, screws, or other non­permanent fasteners) over bonded con­
nections (such as poured, welded, or other chemical or fixed methods that create a permanent bond)
[11].
Technically DfD buildings can be disassembled in the exact opposite way they are constructed as
shown in Figure 2.4. The process of preparing the joints will vary depending on the connection
types. The dismantling process must be carefully planned and safety measures carefully considered
in all scenarios, adding temporary supports to ensure stability etc. [12].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
4
==PAGE:14==
Figure 2.4: Demonstration of dismantling process for a two storey building [12]
One of the advantages of DfD is the possibility to replace only parts of the building. This could be
useful in case of failure of one element for example.
Figure 2.5: Demonstration of fixing damaged beam element [12]
Precast concrete buildings can be designed to be adaptable for future functional changes, such as
transitioning from residential to commercial use, while also allowing for easier upgrades if the
connections are designed to be easily changeable.
Figure 2.6: Upgrade process [12]
A key concept in DfD is the use of prefabricated demountable reinforced concrete (RC) structures,
as highlighted by Fayyad and Abdalqader [13]. These structures utilize dry connections, such as
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
5
==PAGE:15==
dowels, anchor rods, and steel angles that facilitate disassembly with minimal or no destruction.
They emphasize on dry connections being crucial to ensuring that components can be removed and
reused efficiently, as opposed to traditional wet connections, which hinder disassembly due to the
use of mortar or grout [13].
Akbarnezhad et al. [14] emphasize the economic and environmental benefits of DfD compared
to conventional demolition and recycling processes. Traditional recycling often involves crushing
concrete, which is energy­intensive and leads to down cycling, where recycled material is used for
lower­grade applications. In contrast, DfD preserves the embodied energy of building materials by
allowing components to be reused directly, thereby reducing the carbon footprint of construction
through the reuse of structural components. They argue that planning for deconstruction should
begin at the earliest stages of design.
Figure 2.7: Example of DfD connection in BIM environment [14]
To support this Akbarnezhad et al. [14] applied Building Information Modeling (BIM) to eval­
uate deconstruction strategies and assess the life cycle impacts of different approaches on cost,
energy, and carbon emissions. Their framework facilitates the evaluation of the environmental
and economic impacts of deconstruction. The case study finds that although DfD can increase ini­
tial construction costs by up to 43%, it results in significant savings (79%) in the building’s second
life cycle. Over two life cycles, this approach reduces overall energy use and carbon emissions by
35% and 38%, respectively [14].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
6
==PAGE:16==
Figure 2.8: Framework proposed by Akbarnezhad et al. [14]
Küpfer et al. [15] further illustrate the potential of DfD by reviewing 77 cases involving the reuse
of concrete components across Europe and the United States. Their analysis shows that reusing
concrete elements instead of crushing them into aggregates can effectively delay the need for fresh
cement, thus reducing greenhouse gas emissions and mitigating environmental degradation. The
authors argue that the reuse of large concrete elements, when carefully planned can offer significant
sustainability benefits without the need for major reprocessing [15].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
7
==PAGE:17==
Figure 2.9: Example of successful reuse of precast concrete slabs and panels for new construction
near Berlin, Germany [15].
Figure 2.10 illustrates the technical composition of a precast sandwich panel facade system, this
case highlights how addressing weak areas related to transformation capacity and disassembly po­
tential can lead to the development of a new system. Such a system would enhance the disassembly
of parts and components with high exchangeability rates. The separation of the finished compo­
nents and services in this design ensures that material loss and deterioration of the element are
minimized when modifications are required. For instance, new services can be added or existing
ones replaced without damaging the structural integrity. Similarly, when cladding materials be­
come outdated or no longer align with design preferences, they can be disassembled, replaced, and
reused efficiently [6].
Figure 2.10: Facade composition [6]
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
8
==PAGE:18==
Unitized facades, such as those offered by Staticus [16] , provide a highly efficient and modular
alternative to traditional stick facade systems. Unlike stick systems, which are assembled piece
by piece on­site and can be challenging to disassemble, unitized facades are prefabricated off­site
as complete panels. These panels include all necessary components, such as cladding, insulation,
and even glazing, in a single unit [16]. This modular approach not only speeds up installation but
also makes disassembly and reuse easier.
The transition towards DfD, however, is not without challenges. The adaptability of the structural
elements and the need for precise planning at the design stage are critical factors. These include
ensuring that all components are designed with dismantling in mind, maintaining adequate doc­
umentation of materials and connections, and integrating flexibility to accommodate changes in
use. Such challenges highlight the importance of involving architects and engineers who are will­
ing to limit traditional architectural demands for monolithic aesthetics in favor of modular, flexible
designs that can be dismantled effectively ([13]; [14]).
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
9
==PAGE:19==
2.2
Construction methods
Cast­in­situ (or site­cast) concrete is a construction method where concrete is poured, molded,
and cured directly on the construction site. This approach offers flexibility in terms of design and
on­site modifications but requires significant time for formwork preparation, curing, and labor
management. Of course the construction process can be affected by environmental conditions,
which may lead to potential delays.
In contrast, precast concrete involves casting and curing concrete elements in a controlled off­site
environment. Once fully cured, these elements are transported to the construction site and assem­
bled. Precast concrete offers benefits such as higher quality control, faster construction times, and
reduced labor dependency. But it can require careful logistical planning for transport and installa­
tion [17].
2.2.1
Wet connections
A traditional method in precast concrete structures involves using wet connections to create joints
that behave similarly to a monolithic cast­in­situ frame. These joints require formwork and curing
time, which can significantly impact construction speed compared to other precast methods, such as
dry connections [18]. Wet joints are formed by using grout or a similar material to connect different
precast elements, resulting in a monolithic behavior [19], see Figure 2.11. This type of joint ensures
structural continuity, which is critical for overall stability and effective load distribution within
the frame structure [18]. Grouted connections are particularly crucial because they help transfer
compressive forces, allowing elements to work cohesively to bear loads. In addition to grout, wet
joints often include steel reinforcing bars, which help handle tensile forces at the connections [20].
(a) Monolithic connection
(b) Wet connection
Figure 2.11: Comparison of Monolithic and Wet Joint Connections [19]
Wet joints can be categorized into two types: first type is strong connections of limited ductility,
meaning it is strong but not very flexible. These joints are designed so that any bending or yielding
happens somewhere else in the structure, keeping the joint intact. The second type is designed to
be more flexible allowing controlled bending or allow plastic hinges to form at the joint, usually
where a beam connects to a column. This approach allows the structure to behave similarly to a
cast­in­place system under loading [21].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
10
==PAGE:20==
Advantages of Wet joints
There are many advantages of using wet joints. Like mentioned above the in­situ pouring of mortar
into the joints provides a bond between these precast concrete elements and with that improves the
structures ability to transfer loads, such as wind loads across the building.
Ductility is very important for the seismic performance of precast concrete structures. Wet joints
make the structure flexible, allowing it to absorb and release energy during an earthquake. This
flexibility prevents sudden failures and helps the building survive small earthquakes without dam­
age, while larger earthquakes result in only minor, non­structural damage [22]. This makes wet
joints superior to dry joints, which typically have limited ductility and are less effective in seis­
mic regions. Wet joints create a monolithic behavior, enabling effective force transfer across the
connected components. This is particularly beneficial for creating rigid systems that efficiently
transfer forces, such as shear and bending, in a manner that ensures structural stability.
Challenges with wet joints
While wet joints provide structural integrity and ensure that the building behaves as a unified sys­
tem, they present several significant challenges. One of the primary limitations is the need for
on­site curing and form work, which introduces delays in the construction timeline, reducing the
efficiency associated with precast methods. Moreover, wet joints complicate the disassembly pro­
cess, often resulting in complete demolition of structures rather than reusing the undamaged com­
ponents. When a building’s intended purpose changes, the rigid nature of wet joints makes adapta­
tion or repair challenging. Consequently, buildings that could otherwise be structurally sound are
often demolished simply because they cannot be repurposed for a new function. So in short this
traditional building system faces challenges related to adaptability and sustainability [23].
Weaker mortar
Anders Rafn et al. [24] performed research on weaker mortar and their thesis highlights that using
lower strength mortar in wet joints makes sure that the failure happens in the mortar itself or where
the mortar meets the concrete parts. This way the main concrete element is able to stay intact during
disassembly. The difference between regular cement based mortar and the lower strength mortar
tested was that it contains lime which further reduces the strength and stiffness in comparison to
the cement based mortar to make disassembly feasible [24].
Current limitations
To summarize it is clear that there are some limitations in using wet joints. Firstly, there can be
the need for formwork on­site, reinforcement work and cast in situ concrete, which leads to slower
assembly times compared to dry connections. Then, there is the lack of flexibility for disassembly,
unlike dry joints, which are designed for easy removal, wet joints create connections that are often
irreversible, making it difficult to reuse undamaged components. However, it is also clear that
wet joints have certain advantages over dry joints in specific situations and being the preferred
construction method for precast elements. This is why it is interesting to investigate the significant
potential of wet joints and exploring methods to disassemble the elements without damaging them.
2.2.2
Dry connections
This section provides an overview of different types of dry joint solutions commonly used in the
assembly of prefabricated elements. These methods are known for being adaptable and efficient
which makes them beneficial in projects designed with disassembly and reuse in mind.
Dry joints are an alternative for assembling prefabricated elements. They allow easy building
component removal without damaging the structure. Instead of a traditional method of cement­
ing, dry joints offer mechanical connections such as dowels, bolting, post­tensioning, or locking
devices. These connections make assembly and disassembly quick and easy, supporting the reuse
and reconfiguration of building components [25].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
11
==PAGE:21==
Figure 2.12: Example of a dry connection solution [13]
Steel angle connections
One type of dry joint used between precast concrete beams and columns is the steel angle con­
nection. These connections involve steel angles welded with gusset plates and connected using
threaded rods. Aninthaneni et al. [18] tested different configurations of steel angle connections,
such as variations in beam edge distance, duct conditions (grouted or un­grouted), and the type of
fill material used between the connection and the precast elements, to evaluate their impact on load
transfer.
Figure 2.13: Demonstration of the dry joint angle bracket [18]
The experiments involved quasi­static cyclic loading to understand how these configurations affect
stability, slip, and energy dissipation. Results indicated that steel angle connections could achieve
performance similar to monolithic RC frames, provided that specific design parameters were met,
such as ensuring adequate beam edge distance, using non­shrink grout, and applying sufficient
pre­tension to the bolts. These connections provide advantages over customary RC frames. They
allow easy dismantling and reuse of parts and quick damaged part replacement. Which can reduce
downtime and seismic losses. Nonetheless, practical challenges were observed during disassem­
bly, including bolt movement and extra effort needed for grouted ducts, which could pose some
limitations in full­scale implementation [18].
Bolted joints
A common type of dry connection used in precast concrete construction for enabling disassem­
bly and reuse of elements is the bolted joint. These specific wall joints use steel bolts inserted
through holes made during casting using PVC pipes, ensuring accurate alignment. Bolted joints
offer another possible solution for connecting wall panels and other precast components, making
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
12
==PAGE:22==
installation straightforward and allowing for future adjustments. To understand how well these
joints perform under real­world conditions, Cai et al. [26] conducted tests focusing on load re­
sistance, failure mechanisms, and stiffness under cyclic loading. The study showed that factors
like the number of bolts, the strength of the concrete, and how the bolts are tightened significantly
affect the joints’ performance. Failures often occurred due to bolt fractures or concrete crushing
under high loads, which highlights the importance of correct alignment and installation.
Figure 2.14: Demonstration of one of the dry joint bolted connection in a wall [26]
Practical issues, such as bolt slippage and alignment challenges during disassembly, were also
observed. These problems can be addressed by using high­strength bolts, adding reinforcement
to limit movement, and ensuring bolts are properly tightened to prevent slippage [26]. Despite
the benefits of bolted joints for disassembly and reuse, their limitations indicate that load transfer
performance may vary depending on the type of joint used.
Pretension bolt connection
Pretension bolt beam­column connections are an option for precast concrete structures, particularly
in seismic areas. These joints rely on embedded rods, anchor nuts, and pretension bolts to achieve
stability without grout, making them suitable for dry construction. Experimental tests revealed that
these connections exhibit good seismic performance, particularly in terms of deformation capacity
and ductility. The energy dissipation capacity due to the yield of anchor rods was found to be
effective, which helps these connections withstand lateral loads during seismic events.
Figure 2.15: Demonstration of pure dry cast method using pre­tension bolt [27]
The load transfer mechanism in these connections involves anchor rods and threaded bars, which
help distribute forces and maintain stability. During seismic events, the design allows for limited
movement at the connection to accommodate deformation and dissipate energy, reducing the risk
of sudden failure [27].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
13
==PAGE:23==
2.2.3
Hybrid Connections/ Composite
Alternatives and mixing materials, known as composite solutions can also promote circular econ­
omy as some companies aim to demonstrate with their solutions. For instance the Finish company
Peikko which offers variety of precast solutions in connections, beams and innovative technology
mixing timber, steel and concrete [28]. For a company of their size providing solutions all over the
world the research and development is a crucial part. Lately their focus has also shifted towards
more re­usability as stated on their website [29].
”The building industry is responsible for major societal impact, with 40% of total
energy use and 30% of the global amount of waste. The concrete business has a par­
ticularly great responsibility as we account for a large proportion of this. That is why
now have chosen to focus on the environment by converting our products and com­
pany strategy to the Circular Economy. We want the building industry to be part of
the solution and not the problem” [29].
Figure 2.16: Demonstration of the solutions provided by Peikko [28]
The Peikko white papers, Dismount and Reuse of Precast Concrete Frame [30] and The Journey
into Circular Economy [31], present a compelling case for the integration of DfD principles into
precast concrete frame projects to promote sustainability in the construction sector. A key highlight
from these documents is the pilot project focused on the frame dismantling and reuse of precast
concrete elements. This project aimed to explore practical solutions for enabling a circular ap­
proach to concrete construction by designing and testing methods to dismount, reuse, and reinstall
precast frame components in new structures.
Figure 2.17: Test frame for disassembly and reassembly by Peikko [30]
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
14
==PAGE:24==
Figure 2.17 showcases the frame used in the project. One half of the structure was reinforced
and grouted according to conventional methods while the other side minimized grouting with dry
joints.
The economic and environmental cost estimation, which was performed for the study case, shows
that there are both economic and environmental benefits and motivation to consider demountability
as a design aspect. In the study case, demountable frames appeared as 35% cheaper and having
about 50% less environmental load than conventional frames, when construction of a new building
is required. Larger scale test and implementation is required for more accurate results [30].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark

==PAGE:25==
2.3
Demolition and dismantling methods
In construction, the most common method of dismantling buildings involves demolition, where
the structure is crushed, and the resulting material is typically used for lower­value products like
road base materials. Most contractors find demolition attractive because it involves lower labor
costs and shorter project times. However, even though this method is economically convenient, it
generates significant waste, with fewer opportunities to reuse or recycle high­value materials [32].
Recycling is a major key to reducing the environmental impact of construction, cutting down waste,
saving resources and lowering carbon emissions. By implementing recycling strategies like reusing
building parts and improving material recovery during demolition the construction industry can
reduce landfill waste and conserve natural resources leading to more sustainable practices [33].
According to Ding [33], the EU Waste Framework Directive, applied in 2008, sets targets that a
large portion of construction and demolition waste to be recycled, with a goal of 70% by 2020. It
also encourages a specific order for dealing with waste first, preventing it and if that is not possible,
then reusing materials, recycling and as a last resort, disposal.
Many strategies have been developed to reduce waste, and the most common one is the waste
reduction hierarchy [34], as shown in Figure 2.18.
Figure 2.18: Waste minimization hierarchy [34]
Therefore, it is crucial to continue advancing research into dismantling methods with the hope
that one day, every element of a building can be fully reused, moving closer to a truly sustainable
and circular construction industry. While current methods offer significant benefits, they still face
limitations, particularly in the reuse of large structural components like concrete and steel.
The main dismantling methods known today include:
2.3.1
Mechanical demolition
The most widely used approach, mechanical demolition, involves the quick destruction of buildings
using heavy machinery or explosives. While fast and cost­effective, demolition generates large
amounts of waste, with most materials being sent to landfills. These methods are not designed for
element reuse in mind.
The wrecking ball is a method for demolishing, a large heavy steel ball that is swung by a crane to
collide with the structure and breaking it apart. The ball can either be dropped vertically or swung
in an arc to create impact [35]. This method is particularly suitable for large­scale projects where
speed is a priority [36].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
16
==PAGE:26==
Machines like hydraulic excavators can quickly reduce a building to rubble, allowing for faster
site clearance. Specialized attachments, such as shears, breakers, and pulverizers, are used to cut
through steel and concrete, facilitating material separation for disposal or recycling [37].
Another technique is controlled implosion, where explosives are strategically placed to weaken key
structural points, causing the building to collapse inward in a controlled manner. This approach is
often used in urban areas to minimize damage to surrounding structures [38].
Figure 2.19: Traditional method for demolishing structures with Excavators [36]
While mechanical demolition is highly efficient, it does present challenges, such as the generation
of dust, noise, and waste, which require proper environmental management [37]. Although it is
labor cost­effective, the operational expenses of heavy machinery and ensuring compliance with
safety and environmental regulations can be significant. Despite these challenges, mechanical
demolition remains a popular choice for large projects due to its speed and overall effectiveness
[36].
2.3.2
Manual demolition and dismantling
Manual demolition, involves dismantling structures by hand using tools such as handheld jackham­
mers, drills, and pry bars. This approach is often used for smaller projects, repairs or situations
that require high precision, such as when working in densely populated areas or preserving specific
architectural features [37]. Unlike mechanical demolition, manual methods allow for a more care­
ful removal of building materials, which can then be salvaged for reuse or recycling, significantly
reducing waste and environmental impact. Dry joints are mostly dismantled with manual handheld
tools requiring special mechanical connections as discussed in section 2.2.2.
(a) Manual demolition [37]
(b) Manual dismantling [31]
Figure 2.20: Manual demolition and manual dismantling of mechanical connection
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
17
==PAGE:27==
2.3.3
Diamond saw cutting
Diamond saw cutting is used to separate the joints of concrete elements. This process will make a
”clean cut” in the concrete and any residual grouting or concrete must be cleaned afterwards from
the elements with pneumatic hammers risking damaging the elements.
Figure 2.21: Diamond saw cutting of hollow core slab [30]
Figure 2.22: cleaning of residual concrete of hollow core slab [30]
The Danish Technological Institute’s project, P(re)cast [39], focused on dismantling and reusing
hollow core slabs. In the project, joints in the hollow core slabs were cut using a diamond saw
mounted on tracks, commonly referred to as a ”wall saw.” This method enables precise and straight
cutting, with the advantage that diamond saws can cut through various materials, including concrete
and rebar. However, one drawback is that this cutting method can damage the elements. Insights
from the project revealed that the saw removed the profiling on the underside of the slabs, as shown
in Figure 2.23. This damage necessitates additional formwork during the reassembly phase, as the
profiling is specifically designed to eliminate the need for formwork between adjacent elements.
Another issue identified in the project was that the amount of time required for cutting significantly
added to the overall cost. Further insights can be found in in the email correspondence in appendix
C.1.
Figure 2.23: Damaged profiling on hollow core slabs [39]
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
18
==PAGE:28==
2.3.4
Hydrodemolition
Hydrodemolition is a method used to remove concrete by using high­pressure water jets, which
can reach pressures up to 3000 bar. This method can selectively or fully remove concrete without
damaging the structure, keeping the rebars intact and rust­free. It creates a good bonding surface
for new concrete and avoids micro­cracks, which are common with methods like jackhammers.
It also reduces noise, dust, and vibrations, making it a safer option for operators, especially in
projects like bridges, tunnels, and dams [40].
Figure 2.24: Hydrodemolition Robot [40]
Conjet has developed specific hydrodemolition robots which are specialized in hydrodemolition
of concrete.
Selective removal involves using high­pressure water jets, up to 1500 bar, to target and remove de­
teriorated sections of concrete while preserving the surrounding material and ensuring the desired
quality level is maintained.
Non­selective removal, also referred to as ”hydromilling,” is utilized when concrete must be re­
moved to a specific depth regardless of its condition. The robot performs this process using high­
pressure water exceeding 1500 bar. [40]. Hydrodemolition is generally used to repair concrete
surfaces but its potential with selective removal is great with the right use cases such as disman­
tling buildings carefully removing the joints keeping elements intact.
Xu and Meng examine how ultra­high pressure water jets fracture concrete through water hammer
and water wedge effects, expanding cracks and inducing micro cracks along aggregate boundaries
[41]. The efficiency of this process depends on aggregate size and strength; smaller aggregates in
mortar allow easier crack propagation due to weaker cement bonds, enabling more efficient dis­
mantling and smoother surfaces, as noted by Liu et al. [42]. In 2023 Philip Skov Halding proposes
the idea of using hydrodemolition along with weak mortar to make it easier to dismantle precast
concrete elements with conventional wet joints. By replacing traditional mortar with weaker lime
cement­based mortar (or other low strength mortar), disassembly becomes much more feasible, es­
pecially when combining mechanical methods like pulling out concrete slabs with mobile cranes.
as Philip highlights, for the weakest mortar a pulling strength of around 45 tons is needed, which is
deemed unsafe by crane pulling for surrounding structure or environment. Hydrodemolition helps
by removing parts of the mortar joints beforehand, which lowers the resistance and makes the dis­
assembly process more controlled and requires less force. Combining crane pulling and partial
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
19
==PAGE:29==
removal of joints with other methods could be much more efficient and safer then using only crane
pulling. Removing mortar along the span of the slabs would reduce the resistance by 63­65% [43].
In an interview with the company Conjet (appendix B.1), it was suggested that for removing mortar
in between joints Robot 101 Nalta would be the most suitable robot for the job. Conjet’s Robot 101
Nalta is the world’s smallest hydrodemolition concrete removal robot, designed for selective con­
crete removal in confined spaces inaccessible to other methods. It operates on horizontal, vertical,
and angled surfaces, including ceilings and beneath bridge decks. The Nalta can be remotely con­
trolled from up to 50 meters away, enhancing operator safety. Its modular design allows for quick
dismantling into lightweight components movable by hand, allowing easy transport and assembly
in tight spaces [44].
Figure 2.25: Hydrodemolition Robot 101 Nalta [44]
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
20
==PAGE:30==

2.4.1
Safety considerations
To ensure structural stability during disassembly, it is essential to plan and execute the process
in a controlled sequence. This involves carefully analyzing the structural dependencies and load
paths of the building to identify which components must remain intact until specific elements
are removed. Temporary supports, such as props or bracing systems, may be required to prevent
collapse or unbalanced loading on the remaining structure. Additionally, the sequence should be
designed to minimize dynamic loads or vibrations that could destabilize the structure. A detailed
disassembly plan, including structural assessments and safety protocols, ensures that each step
maintains stability and protects both workers and the surrounding environment.
Proper lifting equipment is also required in the reuse process particularly because of safety. The
handling, lifting, and dismantling of precast concrete elements are critical components of construc­
tion and demolition processes.
Lifting clamps for hollow core slabs are often used in the assembly phase. They are quick and easy
to use but could be unsafe when lifting reused hollow core slabs since the mechanisms relies on
the profiling on the side wall which can be damaged or cut away as mentioned in section 2.3.3 for
diamond cutting.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
21
==PAGE:31==
Figure 2.26: Hollow core lifting clamp [45]
Alternatively, fixed cast in anchor positions would be preferable for safer dismantling. Two well
established manufactures, Halfen [46] and Peikko [47], provide comprehensive solutions for secure
and efficient handling of precast elements, further illustrated in the figures below.
Figure 2.27: Example of different types of anchors [48]
Figure 2.28: Threaded lifting rod [46]
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
22
==PAGE:32==
Figure 2.29: Illustration of balanced lifting conditions [47]
The manufacturers provide details in the technical manuals for designers to put into specific lo­
cations to maintain balance and safety without exceeding elements capacities. Mass produced
elements in similar standardized sizes such as hollow core slabs utilize reusable molds where the
location is fixed. Preferably when thinking about DfD the lifting systems should be in standardized
locations but if cut­outs or specific designs are made the center of gravity changes which would
alter the positions [47].
The anchors can be covered with low strength mortar through the building life cycle to be easily
accessible or covered with steel plates or plastic covers designed to fit in the recess.
Concluding, the chosen design, construction method, and dismantling method all play their part in
the effectiveness, safety and re­usability of the elements.
2.5
Potential of Material Reuse in Construction
In the article ”Ombruk av hulldekker” published on October 15, 2024, by Resirqel [49], the com­
pany details their involvement in the dismantling and repurposing of 23 hollow core slabs (HD265)
from a building in Vinterbro, Norway. Resirqel led the process, including mapping, dismantling,
and facilitating the sale of these slabs. The dismantling was carried out by Steens Montering og
Demontering AS, while Stabil AS handled quality assurance and recommended testing procedures
for reuse. The slabs have been transported to a new construction site for integration into an upcom­
ing project. This initiative underscores the importance of cost­effective dismantling and selecting
experienced contractors to achieve efficient reuse of building materials. Resirqel mentions that
the reuse process becomes both better and cheaper with more experience, and now cost­effective
dismantling of hollow core slabs for reuse is a reality [49]. In Norway, resource mapping of ma­
terials is obligatory before a demolition project starts according to TEK17 (Norwegian building
law.). Further info in appendix C.2.
A report was released in 2024, The Boost Reuse project, conducted by Vad ­ Netsocietal, Smith
Innovation, and Molio ­ ConTechLab [50]. The project addresses challenges and unlocks oppor­
tunities to improve the reuse of construction materials in the Danish building industry. One of
the major challenges is the difficulty in understanding and implementing circular business mod­
els, mainly because of the lack of knowledge and the right tools. Another challenge is uncertainty
around how long reusable materials will last, their quality and the difficulty in organizing and
transporting them effectively. Economic challenges also make it harder to adopt reusable materi­
als. The high cost of keeping records and documentations can often discourage use, hard to manage
data on materials and difficult to keep information centralized and up to date. There is not enough
motivation for investors, as buildings are not designed to be taken apart easily [50].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
23
==PAGE:33==
Figure 2.30: Challenges and Uncertainties [50]
Despite all of these challenges the Boost Reuse project identifies several promising opportunities.
For example selective demolition and resource tracking along with early­stage waste management
plans and centralized records, can all help make reusing building materials much more accessible.
Development in the market also holds great potential, to make the reuse of building materials
desirable and motivating, it is essential to have free platforms that connect suppliers and buyers of
reused materials, ensuring strong partnerships across the value chain. The value chain is a series
of activities that a company or an industry undertakes to take a product or service from concept to
delivery to the customer. Another way to motivate stakeholders to adopt reuse could be through risk
funds, tax benefits and bonus schemes. Additionally innovation and regulations such as developing
standards, certifications and circular requirements would help [50].
Figure 2.31: Solutions and opportunities [50]
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
24
==PAGE:34==
2.5.1
The role of Artificial intelligence in Sustainable construction
While the reuse of materials is an important path towards sustainability, the integration of ad­
vanced technologies for example artificial intelligence (AI) offers a huge potential in optimizing
construction and demolition processes [51].
Figure 2.32: Overview of AI Applications in Construction Phases [51]
AI­based systems can enhance the precision, sustainability and decision making through various
phases of a buildings life cycle. Additionally the systems can improve efficiency and safety in the
demolition process for example with advanced image recognition and predictive analytical tools
that can identify and classify the materials during demolition. By analyzing the 3D models and all
the material properties AI can streamline the planning and execution of a deconstruction project.
With doing that it would be possible to simplify workflows, at the same time improve accuracy
and reduce the need for manual labor. AI tools can as well be usefull for image recognition during
inspections of existing structures, they can analyze images to assess potential damage and estimate
the building’s condition. By having efficient material tracking and reuse a more centralized data
management system could be set up powered by AI that would allow companies to be able to mon­
itor reusable elements across projects. This digital platform would also lead to a better connection
between suppliers, buyers and contractors making it easy to find and trade reusable material [51].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
25
==PAGE:35==
Of course there are some challenges when it comes to adopting the AI system into the construction
industry. That is mainly due to the lack of access to structured data. While there is a lot of data
available for AI training only 4% of it is related to construction, which makes it difficult for the
AI models to give precise and valuable output that can be relied on. That is why it is important to
address this challenge and so hopefully companies will invest in improving data quality [51].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
26
==PAGE:36==
3
Circular Construction
As discussed in Chapter 2, the cradle­to­cradle model lays the foundation for circular construction
by promoting the continuous reuse and recycling of materials, reducing waste and environmental
harm. Circular construction reduces resource use and waste by reusing and recycling materials
and building parts. It starts at the design stage, focusing on energy efficiency and future recycling.
Repairing and reusing materials lowers carbon emissions and conserves resources, supporting a
circular economy. Collaboration and innovation across the value chain are key to this shift [52].
Figure 3.1: The Life Cycle of a Building in Circular Construction [52]
3.1
A platform for Circular Solutions
In 2021 the platform Circue was established to help improve the construction industry by advanc­
ing circular practices. Using innovative digital tools, Circue will give building owners valuable
insights that support material reuse and reduce environmental impact. The goal is to help stake­
holders make informed decisions that lead to more sustainable construction processes. Through
technology and collaboration, Circue supports the transition to a circular economy, where buildings
are designed and operated with minimal impact on the environment, in line with global sustain­
ability goals [53].

To gain a deeper understanding of the Circue Platform, and its development and functionality, some

Platform Accessibility
Circue plans to launch a beta version of the platform in a few months in 2025. Initially, it will

needs.
User Adoption Strategy
It was highlighted that the main incentive for users is the platform’s ability to simplify the process
of understanding and managing embodied carbon, embedded resources, and circular potentials
in building portfolios. Most data is pre­provided by Circue, minimizing the effort required from
users, who primarily need to validate the information.

necessary.
Market integration
The platform is being developed in collaboration with a range of stakeholders, including software
developers, building owners, architects, contractors, and demolition companies. These partner­
ships are helping integrate the platform into existing workflows while addressing industry chal­
lenges through real­world applications.
3.1.2
Circue’s Approach to Circular Construction
Circue’s main goal is to achieve circular construction and in order to do that their main focus is
on overcoming the key challenges in the industry, for example the lack of material knowledge,
uncertainties around risk and responsibility, lack of documentation and traceability and the gap in
collaboration and communication between key actors in the construction and reuse process. With
this digital platform that uses innovative tools Circue will be able to enhance decision making
and collaboration. Circue plans to launch tools like automated resource mapping for investment
decisions, a digital building and material database to ensure transparency, and a marketplace for
secondary materials to streamline recycling. This project aims to reduce building demolitions,
encourage renovation, transformation and there by boost the use of recycled materials [54].

• Resource identification: This tool will automatically map all materials and components in

analyses.
• Material value and CO2 footprint: This will provide insight into the materials and its
potential value for reuse or recycling. Also their associated carbon footprint helping with
sustainable planning.
• Decision support: All this will help the building owners evaluate whether renovation, trans­
formation or demolition is the best course of action.

==PAGE:39==
3.2
Non­Destructive Testing Methods
Non­Destructive Testing (NDT) refers to techniques to evaluate the properties and condition of
materials without causing any damage. When it comes to structural concrete reuse, NDT meth­
ods can assess the quality, mechanical properties, and durability of elements while still in place
making sure they meet safety and performance standards. Using this method to identify defects
such as voids, cracks, or corrosion, circular construction is being supported and, again, is promot­
ing reuse. NDT techniques include wave­based, electromagnetic, electrochemical, thermal, and
imaging methods, each suited to perform a specific task like detecting defects or assessing cor­
rosion risks. This chapter examines several NDT methods and how they can provide important
information for reusing structural concrete and improve safety and sustainability in construction
[55].
Ottosen et al. [55] conducted a comprehensive literature review in their study titled ”Non­Destructive
Testing for Documenting Properties of Structural Concrete for Reuse in New Buildings”. Using
DTU FindIt, they identified 19 relevant NDT methods. Based on the number of review papers
discussing them, the methods were evaluated and by doing that offered insight into which tech­
niques are well researched and which are less established. As seen in the Figure 3.3 below, it helps
to understand which methods are more commonly used or known about. Methods like ultrasonic
testing and rebound hammers were discussed in a larger number of review papers, suggesting they
are well established and widely studied.
Figure 3.3: Number of review papers discussing each of the 19 identified NDT methods, high­
lighting research focus [55]
When it comes to reusing elements such as structural concrete components, documenting the char­
acteristics is extremely important to assess the durability, mechanical properties and reinforcement
condition without causing damage to the element. That is were NDT methods come in handy and
allows for effective material reuse. This promotes sustainability and trust in repurposed building
materials.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
30
==PAGE:40==
3.2.1
Wave­based techniques
Ultrasonic testing is a well known NDT method that evaluates the characteristics of concrete by
analyzing the behavior of ultrasonic waves. This method detects flaws like cracks and voids and
estimates the compressive strength, elasticity, and homogeneity by measuring the wave velocity. It
is especially useful for determining homogeneity, which helps pinpoint regions that might be less
homogeneous, such as sections with voids, cracks, or uneven aggregate distribution. Additionally,
it can detect degradation, that is, reveal locations where environmental conditions have impacted
the concrete quality or structural integrity due to loading or aging. Also, estimating the thickness
of damaged layers, such as those affected by fire. Ultrasonic testing can also assist in locating
corrosion damage in embedded reinforcement [55, 56].
Acoustic Emission (AE) testing is an NDT method that monitors the release of transient elastic
waves generated by the sudden redistribution of stress within a material. This technique is espe­
cially useful for detecting cracks, delamination, or other forms of damage in concrete structures.
By capturing acoustic signals emitted during crack spreading or the development of other flaws,
AE testing helps identify areas undergoing structural changes [55, 57].
Impact­Echo testing detects flaws such as voids, delamination, and honeycombing by creating
stress waves in the concrete with mechanical impacts. This method also assesses the structural
integrity and determines the thickness of the concrete by examining the reflected waves [55, 58].
3.2.2
Electromagnetic Techniques
Ground Penetrating Radar (GPR) is an NDT method that uses electromagnetic waves to evalu­
ate the internal structure of concrete. It identifies steel reinforcement, estimates rebar dimensions,
and detects internal defects such as voids, cracks, and delamination. Additionally, GPR can eval­
uate water content and material interfaces, emphasizing corrosion­prone locations and providing
important durability insights [55, 59].
Covermeter is a simple and effective tool for locating steel reinforcement in concrete. It measures
the size and position of rebars and determines the depth of the concrete cover, which is crucial for
evaluating load­bearing capacity and durability. [55].
3.2.3
Physical methods
Rebound hammer another commonly used NDT method, which is mainly used to assess the
surface hardness of concrete. This technique is based on the principle that the rebound of a spring­
loaded hammer correlates with the surface hardness, which can be indirectly related to the com­
pressive strength of the concrete. By measuring the rebound value, it is possible to estimate the
material’s strength and assess its condition [55, 60].
3.2.4
Thermal Methods
Infrared Thermography (IRT) uses infrared cameras to identify temperature changes on the
surface of concrete components. By using this method flaws like delamination, voids and cracks
can reveal information about both subsurface and surface problems [55, 61].
Thermal Conductivity measures moisture change affecting durability [55].
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
31
==PAGE:41==
3.3
Combining Circue’s platform with Disassembly Methods and
Non­Destructive Testing
The dismantling of precast concrete structures involves several key steps, ensuring both safety and
efficiency. The process can vary depending on the structural design, the materials used, and the
dismantling method chosen. Dismantling the structure requires all other parts of the building, such
as ”skin,” ”stuff,” etc., to be removed beforehand.
Having explored in the previous sections the many important steps required to achieve effective
reuse of concrete elements, it is also essential to understand how these steps complement one
another to ensure an easy, seamless, and sustainable process. To better visualize this, Figure 3.4
was created as a potential guide for a process that would achieve circular construction. Each step
of the construction industry is outlined, as well as each step in the process that contributes to
sustainability and the success of circular construction.
Figure 3.4: Circular Workflow for Building Element Reuse and Recycling
The process starts at the beginning with designing for disassembly and creating a comprehensive
plan. This stage is critical to make sure that it will be easy to dismantle and reuse elements at the
end of a building’s life cycle.
Below is a comprehensive explanation of the dismantling phases, integrating the workflow for
achieving circular construction while using hydrodemolition as the main dismantling method.
Preparation Phase
The preparation phase is critical to ensure a safe dismantling process while setting the foundation
for circular construction. It involves:
• Design for disassembly: Ensure the structure includes features like wet joints with weak
mortar to simplify dismantling.
• Assessment of the structure: A detailed inspection of the precast structure is conducted to
assess its stability, identify any risks, and determine the most suitable dismantling method.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
32
==PAGE:42==
• Planning and logistics: This includes preparation of a dismantling plan, defining the se­
quence of operations, equipment required, and safety measures, as well as considering en­
vironmental impact and waste management strategies.
• Documentation of materials: Gather detailed information about materials and components,
documenting it in a material passport or digital platform to enable transparency and future
reuse planning.
• Safety measures: Establish safety protocols and procedures, including the use of personal
protective equipment (PPE) and securing the site.
Setup Phase
The setup phase ensures that the necessary equipment, temporary supports, and infrastructure are
in place before beginning the dismantling operations. This phase involves:
• Setup of cranes: The crane setup is carefully planned to ensure the safety and efficiency of
the lifting operations, taking into account the weight and dimensions of the elements to be
removed.
• Temporary bracing under slabs and walls: Install supports such as bracing or shoring
beneath precast slabs and walls to ensure stability during the dismantling process and prevent
unintended movement or collapse.
• Concrete milling of screed to expose joints: Where hollow core slabs are used with top
layers, perform concrete milling to remove screed layers and expose mortar joints for easier
disconnection.
• Marking of critical areas: Clearly mark connection points, weak joints, and critical areas
to guide the dismantling process and minimize risks.
• Non­Destructive Testing (NDT): Use NDT methods such as ultrasonic testing or ground­
penetrating radar to evaluate the condition of structural elements.
• Verification of structural integrity: Check the remaining precast elements for structural
stability, addressing any signs of instability with temporary reinforcements.
• Material passport integration: Record material properties, condition, and potential appli­
cations into a digital material passport for tracking and reuse.
Dismantling Phase
Hydrodemolition is employed as the primary dismantling method, ensuring safe and efficient sep­
aration of structural components without compromising their integrity. This phase involves:
• On­site setup: Specialized operators arrive, set up equipment, and investigate the job site.
• Removal of joints using hydrodemolition: Fully or partially remove joints with hydrode­
molition robots. For partial removal, separate the remaining joints using crane pulling. The
required level of joint removal is determined beforehand.
• Rebar removal: Remove loose rebar by hand or cut it with an angle grinder as needed.
• Lifting and removal of elements: Cranes or other lifting devices are used to lift elements
and remove them from the structure.
• Evaluation of dismantled elements: Assess the dismantled elements for quality and suit­
ability for reuse, recording findings in the material passport.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
33
==PAGE:43==
Post­Dismantling Phase
Once the dismantling process is complete, steps are taken to prepare the elements for reuse or
recycling. This phase includes:
• Site cleanup: Clear debris and sort materials for recycling or disposal during and after dis­
mantling.
• Waste management: Separate materials like concrete, metals, and insulation for proper
disposal, recycling, or reuse.
• Final inspection: Conduct a final inspection to ensure safety and readiness of the site or
structural elements for further use.
• Transport and storage: Transport elements to storage facilities or directly to new building
sites, ensuring proper handling to maintain quality.
Integration with Circue’s platform: At each step, upload data about materials, methods, and
decisions to Circue’s platform. This ensures transparency, facilitates collaboration, and acts as a
marketplace for reusable materials.
End Goal – Reuse of Elements: The ultimate goal is to enable efficient reuse of building compo­
nents, reducing the need for raw materials, minimizing CO2 emissions, and fostering a sustainable
construction industry.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
34
==PAGE:44==
4
Case Study
4.1
Introduction
This chapter looks into the structural behavior of a residential building located in West­Amager.
Rambøll provided a case study for this thesis, which forms the basis for analyzing the structural
performance of the building. The primary goal is to carry out a detailed investigation of the joints,
focusing on their behavior under specific load conditions. Based on 2D drawings, a 3D model,
load plans, and material sizes provided by Rambøll, the analysis was simplified by focusing on
approximately half of the building.
Figure 4.1: 3D Model of the case study­building.
The building has a stepped design, with the tallest part in the center reaching 8 stories. On each
side, the height decreases to 7 stories, and at one end, it reduces further to 5 stories. In this chapter,
the analysis focuses on the sections with 7 and 5 stories and can be seen in Figure 4.3.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
35
==PAGE:45==
Figure 4.2: Floor plan of the case study building.
4.1.1
Simplified model
It was decided to simplify the structural model of this case study. While a more detailed model
could offer slightly more precise results, it would also add unnecessary complexity and require
more time without providing significant benefits. A simpler model makes it easier to identify the
critical load paths and understand the overall structural response. It also reduces the risk of small
inaccuracies in assumptions or input data having too much influence on the results.
Figure 4.3: 3D Model of the case study­building (Dimensions in meters).
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
36
==PAGE:46==
This simplified model includes all shear walls within the selected section and assumes a rigid
diaphragm action for the floor plate.
The total length of the section is 41.3 meters, and the width is 13.7 meters. The ground floor has
a height of 5 meters, while each of the remaining floors has a height of 3 meters.
4.1.2
Dimensions and layout of walls
The structural layout of the building is designed with shear walls, as highlighted in the floor plan
shown in Figure 4.5 and Figure 4.7 below. These walls provide stability to the structure by resisting
lateral forces, primarily wind loads, acting in both longitudinal and transverse directions. The walls
also carry compressive stresses from vertical loads, such as self weight from the structural elements
and live loads and ensure force transfer to the foundation.
For the ground floor, there are 40 walls defined as stabilizing walls. Of these, 30 span in the y­
direction, which aligns with the shorter side of the building, and 10 span in the x­direction, which
aligns with the longer side.
From the 1st floor to the 4th floor, there are a total of 30 stabilizing walls on each floor. Of these, 20
span in the y­direction, and 10 span in the x­direction. The reduction of 10 walls in the y­direction
compared to the ground floor is due to fewer openings in these walls, eliminating the need to split
them into smaller sections.

4.1.3
Basis
Codes and standards
The design of this structure follows the Eurocodes and their corresponding Danish Annexes.
• Basis of Structural Design: DS/EN 1990 [62]
• Densities, Self­weight, Imposed Loads: DS/EN 1991­1­1.2007 ­ Part 1­1 [63]
• Actions on Structures Exposed to Wind: DS/EN 1991­1­4.2007 ­ Part 1­4 [64]
Consequence Classes
The consequence class of the building is determined based on DS/INF­1990_2024 Consequence
classes for building constructions. The building is classified as a CC3 (high consequence) structure.
According to Eurocode DS/EN 1990:2007, consequence class CC3 corresponds to reliability class
RC3. According to Table B3, the KFI factor for actions associated with RC3 is 1.1. Therefore,
KFI = 1.1 is applied in the calculations to account for the higher reliability requirements of the
building.
Figure 4.9: Consequence class DS/INF­1990_2024 [65]

==PAGE:49==
4.2
Load Analysis
4.2.1
Horizontal load definition
The horizontal load acting on the building is primarily due to wind, which is the dominant lateral
load in Denmark. This is because the region experiences minimal seismic activity. The wind
load calculations are carried out according to DS/EN 1991­1­4, which provides guidelines for
determining wind actions on structures based on the local wind climate and building geometry.
The wind pressure on the facade was calculated and applied as a line load at each floor level of the
building.
The mean wind velocity Vm(Z) at a height Z above ground level is determined based on terrain
roughness and orography, as defined in DS/EN 1991­1­4. The expression used is:
Vm(Z) = cr(Z) · co(Z) · Vb
Where:
• cr(Z) is the roughness factor, which accounts for the variability of wind velocity due to the
height above ground level and the ground roughness of the terrain upwind.
• co(Z) is the orography factor, taken as 1.0 for flat terrain.
• Vb is the basic wind velocity, calculated as:
Vb = cseason · cdir · Vb,0
The basic wind velocity Vb is determined with Vb,0 = 24 m/s, cseason = 1.0, and cdir = 1.0. For
the given location, the terrain category is classified as Category II (low vegetation with isolated
obstacles), with a roughness length z0 = 0.05 m.
The peak velocity pressure qp(Z) at height Z is calculated using:
qp(Z) = [1 + 7 · Iv(Z)] · 1
2 · ρ · V 2
m(Z) = 1.09 kN/m2
Where:
• ρ is the air density, assumed to be 1.25 kg/m3.
• Iv(Z) is the turbulence intensity at height Z.
• Vm(Z) is the mean wind velocity at height Z.
For the calculation, the turbulence intensity and roughness factors are taken according to the rec­
ommendations for Terrain Category II.
The wind pressure acting on external surfaces, We, is calculated as:
We = qp(Ze) · cpe
Where:
• qp(Ze) is the peak velocity pressure at the reference height Ze.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
40
==PAGE:50==
• cpe is the pressure coefficient for external surfaces, determined from the geometry of the
building and windward/leeward face exposure.
Figure 4.10: Wind Load Zone Definitions for Building Facades and Roofs [64]
Figure 4.11: Building Plan with Defined Coordinate System (X and Y Axes) and Wind Load Zones
(D and E)

These are based on standardized parameters from DS/EN 1991­1­1, categorized by occupancy
and usage and data from Rambøll.

4.3
Load Combinations
The load combinations are determined in accordance with DS/EN 1990, which specifies the Ul­
timate Limit State (ULS). The design load, Ed, is calculated by combining permanent loads, a
dominant (leading) variable load, and accompanying variable loads, as shown in the general ex­
pression:
Ed = γG · Gk + γQ · Qk,1 +
!
i>1
ψ0,i · γQ · Qk,i
(4.1)
Where:
• Gk: Characteristic permanent load.
• Qk,1: Leading variable load.
• Qk,i: Accompanying variable loads.
• γG: Partial factor for permanent loads.
• γQ: Partial factor for variable loads.
• ψ0,i: Combination factor for accompanying variable loads.
In accordance with DS/EN 1990, only one leading variable load is applied at full intensity within
each load combination. Other variable loads are included as accompanying loads, reduced using
combination factors (ψ0), to account for the reduced probability of simultaneous peak intensities.
The critical load cases analyzed include:
• Maximum normal force on ground floor: Wind acts as the leading load, accompanied by
the full vertical self­weight and reduced live loads.
• Maximum normal force on top floor: Wind acts as the leading load, accompanied by the
full vertical self­weight and reduced live loads.
• Minimum normal force on top floor: Wind remains the leading load, but with minimal
vertical forces.
• Maximum horizontal force on facade: Wind remains the leading load with no vertical
forces.
The purpose of these load combinations is to help determine the reaction forces in the walls. These
reaction forces are essential for analyzing the behavior of the joints under extreme load conditions,
both for maximum and minimum normal forces.
It is important to note that the wind pressure acts downward only on the roof slab. For intermedi­
ate floors, vertical loads are derived solely from self­weight and accompanying live loads, while
horizontal wind forces act on the walls. To determine the total vertical load on the bottom wall, the
loads of all floors, including the roof, are summed floor by floor, incorporating self­weight, live
loads, and any accompanying effects from wind pressure on the roof.
For the structural analysis, vertical loads are calculated as cumulative values from all floors, while
horizontal wind forces are derived based on the governing wind zones and applied simultaneously
to the structural model.
Calculations for load combinations can be found in Appendix. D.3
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark

==PAGE:53==

4.4.1
Wind Leading Load (Walls, Zones D and E)
For the walls, wind acts horizontally as the leading load. The design surface load is calculated
using the combined wind pressure from zones D and E. The values of Qk,wind (1.58 kN/m2 for
the y­direction and 1.53 kN/m2 for the x­direction) include the effects of external wind pressure
and internal pressure (e.g., underpressure).
Ed = KFI · γQ · Qk,wind
Ed = KFI · γQ · Qk,wind = 1.1 · 1.5 · 1.58 = 2.61 kN/m2
(y­direction)
(4.2)

(x­direction)

Height of the influence zone is calculated as:
• Hinfluence = h1+h2
2
:
Figure 4.12: Wind Load Influence Zones and Equivalent Heights for Each Floor Level
The influence zone shows how much of the building’s height each floor ”takes” for wind load. It’s
found by splitting the height between two floors in half—half from the floor above and half from
the floor below. For example, the ground floor gets half of its own height plus half of the first
floor’s height, which makes its influence zone bigger since the ground floor is taller.
The corresponding linear load acting on each floor is determined by multiplying the design surface
load by the height of the influence zone. The following cases are considered:
Case 1: Top floor (influential zone = 1.5 m):
Fline,Y = Qk,wind · Hinfluence = 2.61 · 1.5 = 3.92 kN/m
Fline,X = Qk,wind · Hinfluence = 2.52 · 1.5 = 3.78 kN/m

Fline,X = Qk,wind · Hinfluence = 2.52 · 3 = 7.55 kN/m
Case 3: Ground floor (influential zone = 4 m):
Fline,Y = Qk,wind · Hinfluence = 2.61 · 4 = 10.45 kN/m
Fline,X = Qk,wind · Hinfluence = 2.52 · 4 = 10.10 kN/m

Figure 4.13 shows how the wind load is converted into line loads at each floor level. These values
vary based on the height of the influence zones, with larger loads occurring at the ground floor,
and smaller at the top floor.
4.4.2
Reaction force in stabilizing walls
Now that the horizontal loads have been defined, the next step is to analyze the reaction forces
in the stabilizing walls through a detailed model created in Robot Structural Analysis. To ensure
accuracy, these results are compared to hand calculations performed using the rigid floor method.
4.4.3
Rigid Floor Method
The rigid floor method was used to calculate the reaction forces in the stabilizing walls under hori­
zontal wind loads. This method assumes that the floor diaphragms act as infinitely rigid horizontal
planes, distributing the wind load to the stabilizing walls based on their stiffness and position. The
stabilizing walls are assumed to be consistently placed across all floors and fixed to the foundations,
allowing them to resist horizontal forces effectively.
The analysis considers only the stabilizing walls to resist the wind loads, even though other struc­
tural components, such as floors and facades, also contribute. A detailed explanation of the rigid
floor method can be found in Appendix D.4.
The results for the top floor are summarized below.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
45
==PAGE:55==
Figure 4.14: Shear walls on the top floor
Figure 4.15: Reaction Forces in the Walls Due to Wind Load from the Y Direction
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
46
==PAGE:56==
Figure 4.16: Reaction Forces in the Walls Due to Wind Load from the X Direction
The results indicate that Wall 1 and Wall 3 carry the largest reaction forces in the Y ­direction,
primarily due to their higher relative stiffness and location in the structure. In the X­direction,
Wall 1 and Wall 7 have the highest reaction force. The distribution of reaction forces among the
remaining walls aligns with their relative stiffness and spatial placement. The sum of the reaction
forces matches the applied external loads, confirming that the system is in equilibrium.

4.5
Vertical load analysis
4.5.1
Wind Leading Load on ground floor (Residential Floor)
For residential floors, wind acts as the leading load, with accompanying vertical loads from self­
weight and reduced live loads. The design load is calculated as:
Ed = KFI · (γG · Gk,str + γG · Gk,oth + γQ · Qk,wind + ψ0 · γQ · Qk,live)
(4.4)
Substituting the values:
Ed = KFI · (γG · Gk,str + γG · Gk,oth + γQ · Qk,wind + ψ0 · γQ · Qk,live)
= 1.1 · (1.0 · 3.5 + 1.0 · 3.9 + 1.5 · 0.0 + 0.5 · 1.5 · 2.0)
= 1.1 · (3.5 + 3.9 + 0.0 + 1.5)
= 1.1 · 8.9
Ed = 9.79 kN/m2
4.5.2
Wind Leading Load on top floor (Roof)
For the roof, wind acts as the leading load, with accompanying vertical loads from self­weight and
reduced live loads. The design load is calculated as:
Maximum normal force applied
Ed = KFI · (γG · Gk,str + γG · Gk,oth + γQ · Qk,wind + ψ0 · γQ · Qk,live)
(4.5)
Substituting the values:
Ed = KFI · (γG · Gk,str + γG · Gk,oth + γQ · Qk,wind + ψ0 · γQ · Qk,live)
= 1.1 · (1.0 · 3.2 + 1.0 · 3.4 + 1.5 · 0.52 + 0.0 · 1.5 · 1.0)
= 1.1 · (3.2 + 3.4 + 0.78 + 0.0)
= 1.1 · 7.38
Ed = 8.12 kN/m2
Minimum normal force applied
With wind suction
Ed = KFI · (γG · Gk,str + γG · Gk,oth + γQ · Qk,wind + ψ0 · γQ · Qk,live) = 2.7kN/m2
(4.6)
