5
Autodesk Inventor Analysis
Autodesk Inventor was used to analyze the performance of weak mortar joints, with the goal be­
ing to select the optimal mortar class that ensures structural integrity but also allows for easier
disassembly. The software enables precise modeling and simulation of structural behavior un­
der various loading conditions, allowing for a detailed evaluation of the weak mortar’s ability to
transfer stresses without joint failure.
5.1
Mesh Optimization and Accuracy Validation
The purpose of this accuracy check was to ensure that the finite element mesh used in our Autodesk
Inventor models would provide reliable results. The mesh subdivides the model’s geometry into
smaller elements, allowing the software to perform complex simulations by solving equations at
these discrete points.
There are several settings to control the quality and accuracy of simulations. To determine the
optimal meshing strategy, a small test model was created to analyze how mesh settings impact
stress results. The test involved a simple beam subjected to a 10 kN load applied at its edge.
Results from both hand calculations and Autodesk Inventor simulations were compared. Hand
calculations predicted a maximum bending stress of 120 MPa. The corresponding results from
Inventor are presented in Table 5.1, both the hand calculations and results from Autodesk Inventor
are provided in Appendix E.
Table 5.1: Mesh Size Sensitivity Analysis for Stress
Mesh size
Inventor Stresses (MPa)
Accuracy (%)
0.1
114.2
95%
0.09
117.2
98%
0.08
119.1
99%
0.07
119.1
99%
0.06
121.1
101%
As seen in Table 5.1, the result for a mesh size of 0.1 mm was 114.2 MPa, giving an accuracy of
95%. After refining the mesh, a 0.07 mm mesh size was identified as the best fit, providing an
accuracy of 99%, which is very close to the calculated stress.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
56
==PAGE:66==
Figure 5.1: beam accuracy check ­ mesh size 0.07
Other mesh settings that were looked into was the following:
The minimum element size, that defines the smallest allowable size of mesh elements, as a fraction
of the average element size, so for example when setting it to 0.2 it means that the smallest elements
will be 20% of the average element size. When testing this out with the beam model it did not have
any effect whatsoever it could be better used when working with a larger model but it was decided
to keep it as 0.2 as recommended by Autodesk Inventor.
Grading factor, controls the rate of change in element size between adjacent elements. A smaller
grading factor creates a more uniform mesh, while a larger factor allows larger transitions between
element sizes. When testing this out with the beam model it did not have any effect what so ever,
there for it was decided to keep it as 1.5 as recommended by Autodesk Inventor.
Create curved mesh elements, this enables the generation of curved elements instead of purely
linear elements. Makes sure that curved surfaces in the geometry are better represented. When
trying this out it was concluded that having this box checked gave better numerical precision and
higher­order approximation of stresses.
Figure 5.2: Mesh settings
For the primary models in the following sections, a mesh size of 0.07 and the same mesh settings
as described in Figure 5.2 were used.

5.3
Material properties
In the process of determining the type of mortar strength to examine, DS/EN 1992­1­1 (table 3.1)
[66] was utilized to test normal concrete strengths ranging from C60 to the lowest C12, which
would be placed in the models as the grout or mortar between slabs and walls. For strengths
below C12, specifically C10, C5, and C2.5, the formulas from DS/EN 1992­1­1 were used to
derive the corresponding values. Additionally, results from a test performed at DTU by three
master’s students on shear connections with various mortar strengths were used to examine even
lower strength levels [24]. Table 5.2 adapted from Rafn et al. [24], provides an overview of
the properties of various mortar types that were used in the DTU test on shear connections. For
this study, LC50/50 was chosen for testing in the Inventor model because it represents the lower
boundary of compressive strength in their experiments.
Table 5.2: Overview of mortars used in test at DTU [24]
Cement
Lime­cement
content [­]

[MPa]

[MPa]

16
Some important notes from the results of their test, were it was clear that when a normal force
was applied it increased the friction at the interface of the concrete and mortar. Then it was also
clear that the reinforcement played a crucial role in transferring the forces and preventing sudden
failure. For the cement based mortar C100/400 the reinforcement effectively yields under shear
stress, contributing to the ductility and capacity of the connection. However, with lime­cement
mortars (LC), the lower strength of the mortar does not allow the reinforcement to yield fully,
leading to a less effective load transfer and reduced shear capacity.
In conclusion Cement­Based Mortar has higher compressive and shear strength, making it work
well with reinforcement. It creates a strong interface that allows the reinforcement to fully con­
tribute, increasing the overall shear capacity. Lime­Cement Mortar is weaker and was chosen
to make disassembly easier. However, its lower strength prevents the reinforcement from fully
engaging, which reduces the overall shear capacity.
It is worth mentioning that the use of lime­cement mortar was explored due to its potential to
facilitate disassembly by ensuring failure occurs at the interface rather than damaging the concrete
elements. The lime content causes slower strength development compared to cement­based mortar,
making it less suitable for scenarios requiring rapid load­bearing capacity. However, over time,
its strength continues to increase. The mortar is meant to remain weaker than the surrounding
concrete to allow controlled failure at the joint, but its long­term hardening could make separation
more difficult than intended. This highlights a challenge in using lime­cement mortar, while it
starts off suitable for disassembly, its strength gain over time may undermine its purpose.
Despite these conclusions from the test results, the LC50/50 test results were still used to analyze
the Inventor model. This was due to its low mortar strength, as the aim was to evaluate whether
such low strength could be viable in the joints. It was recognized that for further investigations,
a cement­based mortar or another material combination might be more suitable than lime­based
mortar for achieving the desired balance between strength and disassembly potential.
In Table 5.3 the concrete strengths can be seen that was used for the walls and slabs in all the

5.4
Constraints and applied loads
Constraints
Constraints that are applied in the models are used to try to replicate the real world boundary
conditions of the structure, the goal being that the models behave realistically under applied loads.
Fixed constraints were applied along the base of the wall to prevent both vertical and horizontal
displacements. Additionally, the slab edges were constrained to allow for realistic load distribution
and to simulate pinned supports.

In section 4.4, it is described how the horizontal load was found using Robot Structural Analysis,
and the vertical forces are calculated as described in section 4.5.

5.5
Simulation of Models
To evaluate the performance of the mortar joints, the models were simulated and analyzed with a
focus on understanding how they handle different load components and distribute stresses.
Compression plays a critical role, as most of the load is carried in compression. Mortar generally
performs well under compressive forces due to its inherent strength. However, tension presents a
more significant challenge. Mortar is weaker in tension, and if tensile stresses exceed its capacity,
cracks may form. These cracks can compromise the integrity of the joint by providing a path for
water ingress and potentially causing corrosion in the embedded steel reinforcement. In real­life
applications, however, it can be assumed that most of the tensile stresses in the mortar joints are
carried by the reinforcement. While our model does not explicitly include reinforcement, it was
well understood that when the tensile capacity of the mortar or concrete is exceeded, the embedded
reinforcement will take over and carry the remaining tensile forces.
To ensure the mortar can safely handle both compression and tension, it was crucial to verify the
1st principal stress (tension) and the 3rd principal stress (compression). This verification ensured
that both components remain within the mortar’s capacity and that the structural performance of
the joints will not be compromised.
Stiffness also plays an important role in the behavior of the mortar. A stiffer mortar tends to attract
more load and higher stresses, increasing the likelihood of cracking if the tensile or compressive
capacity is exceeded. Meaning stiffer mortar doesn’t bend or give much under pressure, so it ends
up carrying a larger amount of the load. On the other hand, weaker mortar, bends or deforms
more easily under pressure. Because of this, the surrounding elements, like walls or slabs, take on
more of the load instead. This reduces the stress on the mortar itself and spreads the load more
evenly across the whole structure. This makes them particularly suitable for disassembly purposes
provided their tensile and compressive strengths are not exceeded.
An example of how the mortar is isolated from the model can be seen in the figures below, Fig­
ure 5.10 and Figure 5.11

(b) Mortar isolated

5.6
Results
Table 5.4 presents the stress results for the mortar joints, focusing on the 1st principal stress and
3rd principal stress for models 1 through 4. These results illustrate the stress distribution in the
mortar joints under different structural configurations and material properties, providing insights
into the tensile and compressive capacities of the mortars in each scenario. The mortar types and
results highlighted in red in the table represent the suitable mortar strength to use for each model.
These mortars were selected based on their ability to handle both tensile and compressive stresses
without exceeding their respective capacities. Of course, it must be taken into account that we
are assuming the reinforcement bars will take over when the tensile stress in the mortar exceeds
its capacity. The tensile capacity of reinforcement bars is significantly higher than for the mortar,
with yield strengths commonly going from 400 MPa to 500 MPa in standard reinforcing steel.

Comparison between Model 2 and Model 3
Model 2, without the extra wall, shows higher stress concentrations in the mortar joints as the
load is transferred directly through the slabs. This leads to increased compression and tension in
the mortar, as the wall­to­slab connection bears the majority of the load. In contrast, model 3,
with the addition of an extra wall underneath the slabs, provides additional structural support that
helps redistribute the load more effectively. This redistribution lowers stresses in the mortar joints
and creates a more balanced distribution of compression and tension. The added wall in model 3
improves the overall stiffness and stability of the system, preventing excessive deformation and
reducing stress peaks.
In terms of representing real­world scenarios, model 3 might be a better representation since the
actual structure includes similar additional support. Results for both model 2 and 3 are compared
in Figure 5.12 and Figure 5.13. In Appendix E.4 results for the mortar joints and displacement can
be seen.
(a) Model 2, 1st principal stress
(b) Model 3, 1st principal stress
Figure 5.12: Model 2 and 3 comparison
(a) Model 2, 1st principal stress
(b) Model 3, 1st principal stress
Figure 5.13: Model 2 and 3 comparison
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
68
==PAGE:78==
Comparison between model 2 and model 4
Figure 5.14 compares the 1st principal stress distribution in model 2 and model 4. In model 2, with
a bonded contact type, stresses are concentrated near the wall­to­slab interface, reflecting a rigid
connection where the load is fully transferred through the mortar. The wall and slab are tightly
connected, so the load is forced through a smaller area. This creates higher stress because the
connection is rigid and doesn’t allow any movement or adjustment.
In model 4, the separation/no sliding contact type allows the joints to separate slightly, redistribut­
ing the load more evenly and reducing stress concentration in the mortar. The stress is lower
because the connection is more flexible. Instead of being tightly bonded, the wall and slab can
separate slightly, which changes how the load is transferred. This flexibility helps spread the load
over a larger area, reducing stress at the connection point.
(a) Bonded contact, 1st principal stress
(b) Separation/no sliding contact, 1st principal stress
Figure 5.14: Model 2 and 4 comparison
Figure 5.15 shows the difference in 3rd principal stress similarly to the 1st principal stress the
bonded contact forces the wall and slab to transfer the load directly at the connection, causing high
stress concentrations. In contrast, separation/no sliding contact allows slight movement, spreading
the load over a larger area and reducing stress concentrations for a more even distribution.
(a) Bonded contact, 3rd principal stress
(b) Separation/no sliding contact, 3rd principal stress
Figure 5.15: Model 2 and 4 comparison
Similarly, when isolating the mortar joints, as shown in Figure 5.16 and 5.17, the key difference
between the two models lies in stress distribution. In the bonded contact model 2, stress is con­
centrated more heavily in the mortar joints, while in the separation/no sliding model 4, the stress
is distributed more evenly to the slab and wall, resulting in lower stress levels within the joints.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
69
==PAGE:79==
(a) Bonded contact, 1st principal stress
(b) separation no sliding contact, 1st principal stress
Figure 5.16: Model 2 and 4 comparison
(a) Bonded contact, 3rd principal stress
(b) separation no sliding contact, 3rd principal stress
Figure 5.17: Model 2 and 4 comparison
These results suggest that while model 2 provides a more realistic and stable representation of stress
distribution, model 4 introduces behaviors that may not align with actual load transfer in precast
structures. From the analysis of these models, our recommendation is to use mortar type C10. We
consider this to be on the safer side as it provides a slightly higher strength margin, ensuring that
the tensile capacity of the mortar is not exceeded. Like mentioned above exceeding this capacity
could lead to crack formation, which would allow water to seep into the joints. This poses a risk
of reinforcement corrosion over time, compromising the durability and long­term integrity of the
structure. Table 5.1 in Teknisk Stabi (udgave 21) [67], lists the minimum strength requirements
for concrete in different environmental conditions. For passive (dry) environments, the minimum
strength class is C12/15. Since the mortar is used in internal joints protected from external factors
like rain, frost, and chlorides, it is assumed fully protected. In this setting, C10 is a suitable choice,
providing sufficient performance without exposure to aggressive environmental loads.
The reinforcement was intentionally left out of the models to focus on the mortar as the primary
load carrying connector. Including reinforcement would enhance tensile strength and robustness,
potentially overshadowing the mortar’s performance. By excluding it we simulate a worst case
scenario, ensuring the mortar alone can manage load transfers, both during normal use and dis­
mantling.
Future work, create a test setup in a laboratory to investigate the behavior of a wall­to­slab joint
under transverse loads. Monitor the system for cracking, load distribution, and deformation to
verify the findings of the analysis. Further modeling with reinforcement to capture its contribution
to resisting tension in the system. A more detailed reinforcement model would provide additional
insights but could be a complex study requiring its own thesis.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
70

Cost estimation, CO2 and price comparison
The following section describes the process of comparing methods suitable for demolition in re­
gards to prices, time, and global warming potential (GWP = kgCO2eq). Strength classes compared
are C10, C30 and C60 mortar. Prices and productivity rates are from various sources detailed in
each section and supported by the Danish Molio prisdata [68] price bank as well as Hannarr byg­
gingarlykill [69] which is the Icelandic alternative to Molio.

The operational costs are calculated based on fuel and water consumption: Fuel cost for a pump

(Engine effect 230 kW) estimated consumption is 57.5L/h. Price of diesel assumed 13dkk/L
In Denmark, the average combined cost for drinking water and wastewater services in 2021 was
€9.85 per cubic meter (m³), which includes VAT and other taxes [70]. This equates to approxi­
mately €0.00985 per liter.
The unit requires a water flow rate of 32 liters per minute (L/min) which translates to 1.92 cubic
meters per hour (m³/h). At this flow rate, the cost of water usage would be approximately €0.315
per minute, or 141.75Dkk (€18.90) per hour.

6.3
Diamond cutting demolition
Finish contractor GLEN [73] provides comprehensive pricing for all types of concrete cutting,
including detailed add­on costs such as labor rates per hour, extra equipment rental, and additional
charges for specific conditions. They note that reinforcement can significantly slow down the
cutting process. For reinforcement exceeding 16mm K150, prices increase by 30%. If more than
50% of the cutting surface consists of rebar, the cost can rise up to 10 times higher due to the
increased wear and tear on the blades. Therefore when the saw encounters and needs to split
longitudinal rebar, the cost and time required could increase substantially.
Additionally, the hardness and abrasiveness of aggregates in concrete play a critical role in diamond
cutting efficiency. Harder aggregates reduce cutting speed and cause greater wear on the diamond
blade, while softer/smaller aggregates allow faster cutting and result in less blade wear [74].
Productivity rates and total time for diamond cutting was calculated for the total joints 4364 meters.
Productivity rate for C60 mortar assumed as 3m/hr including setup, moving and preparation based
on Molio no.(23)21.70.02 with the other strength classes marginally faster.

It is worth noting that this analysis does not include several key cost items that drive up the total
costs of the building. It was decided not to include them to simplify calculations and compare
different methods equally. The cost items not included are for example: Site running cost, crane
setup and rental, other labor cost and removal of ”skin” and ”service” elements (cladding, windows,
ventilation etc.).
6.6
CO2 emissions
CO2 emission are measured in various ways with the most common being GWP. Manufacturers
provide detailed results of LCA analysis of their products through Environmental Product Decla­
ration (EPD) according to ISO 14025. EPDs used in this analysis can be found in appendix F.5
LCA stages are divided into 5 main parts,
• A1­A3: Raw material extraction, transport, and production.
• A4­A5: Delivery and construction/installation.
• B1­B7: Use, maintenance, repair, and operational impacts.
• C1­C4: End­of­life processes like demolition, transport, and disposal.
• D: Future benefits from recycling or reusing materials.
This is further illustrated in the figure below,

The table highlights the total costs and associated emissions for the three disassembly approaches.
Of the investigated methods, using hydrodemolition to recover and reuse slab elements proves to
be more cost­effective and environmentally friendly. Specifically, it is up to 11.3% cheaper than
traditional demolition techniques and results in 18.8% lower emissions.
If the end of use phase is isolated and decisions on the disassembly method prioritize minimizing
immediate costs, demolition appears to be the cheapest option. However, as seen in the second
construction phase, the potential gains are already lost.
Based on these results further gains can be found by reusing the walls as well since they account for
roughly half of the structural elements. Optimizing the disassembly process with lower­strength
mortar and selecting the most suitable disassembly methods could further enhance the efficiency,
speed, and cost­effectiveness of the process. These refinements could reduce labor requirements,
energy consumption, and the likelihood of damage to reusable components
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
80
==PAGE:90==
7
Conclusion
A comparative literature review was conducted to evaluate various sustainable solutions for DfD
and construction methods. The review examined a range of approaches aimed at promoting circular
construction practices, reducing waste, and facilitating the reuse of materials. Key areas of focus
included joint designs older research that has laid the groundwork for current advancements in DfD
practices.
This thesis explored dismantling methods for precast concrete element buildings, focusing on im­
proving re­usability and sustainability in construction. By investigating the use of weak mortar
joints and hydrodemolition, we identified effective approaches to preserve the integrity of precast
components during disassembly.
Based on the case study of a real building in Denmark, analyzing load paths, critical walls and
scenarios the mortar joints are investigated further. With Autodesk Inventor, the FEM analysis of
a critical case the joints are found to be able to use C2.5 up to C10 mortar, lowering the strength
from the commonly used C55 or higher with cast in situ joints.
The study also highlighted the possibility of integrating non­destructive testing to evaluate the
condition of structural components before reuse. In addition, platforms such as Circue advance
circular construction by offering tools for resource mapping, material documentation, and market
integration. By improving traceability and collaboration, Circue addresses key challenges and
supports the efficient adoption of dismantling and reuse methods. This ensures compliance with
safety standards and enhances confidence in circular construction practices.
Our analysis demonstrated that weak mortar joints, when paired with selective hydrodemolition,
offer practical solutions for dismantling while minimizing damage to reusable elements. These
methods present promising advantages over traditional demolition techniques, particularly in re­
ducing waste and carbon emissions. The comparison between demolition, hydrodemolition and
diamond cutting found that hydrodemolition was both the cheapest and least polluting option over
2 constructions when using C10 mortar and electric water pump. 11.3% cheaper and 18,8% less
emissions compared to demolition. Further gains are to be found reusing more elements of the
building.
For hydrodemolition further tests are required for the specific case of removing only mortar joints
and optimizing the frame the robot operates on in terms of safety and cleaning the surrounding
area.
This research provides a foundation for advancing dismantling techniques and supports Denmark’s
efforts toward sustainable construction. Future work should prioritize large­scale implementation,
policy development, and industry collaboration to accelerate the shift to a circular economy in the
building sector.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
81
==PAGE:91==
8
Reflections
This thesis has demonstrated the potential of dismantling methods, such as weak mortar joints
and hydrodemolition, to improve the reuse of precast concrete elements. Using wet joints aims to
minimize changes to already known methods of precast element buildings. While the findings are
promising, they also expose several challenges and areas for development.
The figure below shows a test run by Conjet, where they used a Nalta robot on a movable frame.
This setup has a lot of potential. The frame’s design could be optimized to fit and work even better
between the hollow core slab joints or for other specific scenarios.
Figure 8.1: Nalta mounted on a movable frame with lockable wheels ­ test done on parking floor
in Sweden [76]
To address practical concerns, additional features such as a protective covering or tent over the
frame could be implemented. This addition would contain water spray, making the operation
cleaner and safer, while reducing the spread of debris and contaminated water to surrounding areas.
Such measures would simplify the cleanup process.
Future studies could take these findings a step further by setting up a full­scale test scenario to eval­
uate the potential of hydrodemolition with precast elements in a controlled, realistic environment.
Such a test would involve the use of precast concrete elements constructed with weak mortar to
simulate the conditions under which hydrodemolition is expected to perform optimally. By apply­
ing the technique to structural components like hollow­core slabs and wall elements, researchers
could gather valuable data on its efficiency, precision, and impact on element integrity. The test­
ing of weak mortar joints is to fine­tune their design, ensuring they provide the necessary strength
during a building’s lifespan while allowing for efficient disassembly and providing designers with
the necessary tools to evaluate mortar joint strength. One critical aspect of these studies would be
evaluating the cost dynamics over time. As with any innovative technology, initial implementation
costs may be higher due to the learning curve and the need for specialized equipment. However,
with more experience and accumulated knowledge, the process would inevitably become more
streamlined, leading to significant cost reductions. Workers and operators would become more
skilled in using the equipment, optimizing setup and operation times, while companies would adapt
their procedures to maximize efficiency. The increased familiarity with hydrodemolition would
also enhance its precision, minimizing errors and wastage, and enabling more consistent recovery
of high­quality elements.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
82
==PAGE:92==
Another critical area is the market for reused materials. Without clear standards, certifications, and
a robust trading platform, the adoption of reused elements remains limited. Platforms like Circue
provide a promising step toward addressing these gaps by improving transparency and connecting
stakeholders. However, significant work is needed to make reuse an industry norm.
While promising, the adoption of these methods faces challenges, including a lack of standardiza­
tion, limited industry awareness, and the need for early­stage design integration. The absence of
clear guidelines for weak mortar joint applications and hydrodemolition in design and construction
further complicates implementation. Moreover, the construction industry often resists adopting
reuse methods due to higher initial costs, increased manpower requirements, and longer project
timelines compared to traditional demolition and rebuilding approaches.
A significant issue that remains unresolved is the responsibility for reclaimed elements. When a
hollow core slab is cut out, who takes ownership of it? Who perform the necessary tests to make
sure its structural integrity for reuse in a new building? And where will these elements be stored
in the meantime? One potential solution would be for the original manufacturers to reclaim their
elements, perform the required testing, and store them until they are resold and integrated into a
new structure. This approach could help maintain quality assurance and ensure that responsibility
for the elements continues beyond their initial use.
There are many challenges with cutting hollow core slabs and one of it is dealing with the top
concrete layer, which is usually added to create a rigid floor diaphragm and leveling. When the
slabs are cut, this concrete topping layer is still there and it is no longer beneficial for distributing
forces. Removing it with a concrete miller takes extra time and money, but leaving it in place adds
unnecessary weight. It is assumed that in most cases a new concrete topping would have to be
poured over the old one which would reduce the capacity of the element in its second use phase.
Addressing these challenges requires practical solutions, such as developing industry standards,
promoting education on reuse techniques, and offering financial incentives. These steps are crucial
to encourage wider adoption, streamline processes, and make sustainable construction methods
more feasible and appealing to the industry.
Finally, establishing a viable business model for reuse and circular construction is essential. Col­
laboration among stakeholders, including designers, contractors, and policymakers, will be critical
to integrating these methods into practice.
The findings in this thesis highlight the path forward for creating a more sustainable construc­
tion industry, but meaningful progress will require continuous innovation, practical solutions, and
strong industry and policy support.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark

==PAGE:99==
A
Site visit
A.1
HCS diamond cutting ­ Site visit at DTU
Date 10.03.2024
During the literature review phase of our thesis, we had the opportunity to observe a portion of
another master’s student’s project at DTU. Luise Dennin was investigating the potential for reusing
hollow core slabs (HCS) as walls in new construction.
Purpose of visit
• Get a better understanding of how diamond saw cutting works
• Take pictures
• Talk to the worker, ask about the cutting process and the possible conflicts
Findings from the visit
Luise obtained old HCS elements from P. Olsen, a demolition company, which had been dismantled
from an old building. The initial and most relevant phase of her experiment, from our perspective,
involved cutting the HCS. The method used for this was diamond saw cutting. After the slabs were
cut, her next step was to apply an axial load to the repurposed slabs, treating them as ”walls” to
simulate their behavior under vertical loads in a real building scenario. This test aims to evaluate
the structural performance of the reused HCS when subjected to compression forces typical of load­
bearing walls. By applying axial load, Luise could assess factors such as the load­bearing capacity,
deformation, and overall integrity of the hollow core slabs when used in a different structural
configuration.
One interesting observation when we first saw the HCS was that it was clear a diamond saw had
been used to cut the slabs along their width, as can be seen in Figure A.1 (a). However, for the
length of the slabs, it appeared that a different method had been used to separate them, possibly by
forcing them apart or breaking them with a jackhammer, see Figure A.1 (b).
(a) Width of HCS before cutting
(b) Length of HCS before cutting
Figure A.1: HCS before diamond saw cutting
This is really good news for us the fact that less cutting is required, and simpler methods may
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
90
==PAGE:100==
be sufficient for separating the slabs, makes the dismantling process easier, faster, and more cost­
efficient.
When speaking with the worker, it became clear that cutting through mortar is easier than cutting
through concrete. Additionally, cutting through a hollow core slab (HCS) is faster since there is
less concrete to cut through compared to solid concrete elements.
When using a diamond saw, there are several types of machines available. For this experiment, a
floor saw was used. There is also a hand saw and a rail saw, where the saw is mounted on a guide
rail or track, allowing for precise and controlled cuts. The rail saw is the most expensive option
but, as mentioned earlier, provides the most precise and controlled cuts.
The worker also pointed out several safety concerns that need to be addressed when using a di­
amond saw. These include wearing steel­toe boots, earplugs to protect against the loud noise,
and safety glasses. Additionally, on construction sites, other factors must be considered, such as
whether the worker can safely stand on the element while it is being cut and whether the element
is adequately supported to prevent it from falling during the cutting process.
It’s also important to wear a mask to prevent inhaling fine dust generated during cutting. Addition­
ally, bending over for extended periods can strain the back, which is another consideration when
using the saw for prolonged tasks.
Figure A.2: Diamond saw cutting of a HCS
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
91

B
Interviews
B.1
Conjet interview 10.17.2024
With: Ola Wanger, Simon Christander and Conny Tångring
In this interview we got the opportunity to meet with three Conjet employees and ask some im­
portant questions we had on our mind. First we began by explaing our goals and thoughts, that is
we are exploring the feasibility of using Conjet’s hydrodemolition robots for a new application:
removing grout or mortar between precast concrete elements in buildings. The primary goal is to
use a weaker grout or mortar than the concrete slabs, allowing only the mortar to be removed while
keeping the slabs intact, thereby enabling the reuse of building components.
Conjet shared that their hydrodemolition robots have been successfully used in the context of dis­
assembly projects involving precast concrete, and promised to send us some specific cases that
would be relevant to our research.
Regarding our specific interest, Conjet expressed optimism that their robots could be adapted to
remove weak mortar between precast elements without damaging the adjoining concrete. The
feasibility of this application depends on configuring the water pressure and adjusting the width
and depth of the water jet appropriately. Conjet highlighted that achieving precise removal requires
ensuring that the concrete is free of pre­existing cracks, as high­pressure water can infiltrate cracks
and lead to unintended damage. For added precision, they suggested using steel plates as a guiding
template to confine the spread of the water jet.
Water consumption for hydrodemolition depends largely on the project scale and requirements.
The water usage ranges from 20 to 220 liters per minute, which generally requires a pump, as
standard water supply systems are not sufficient. Furthermore, water must be collected and filtered
on­site as part of the process, a solution that varies depending on the location and conditions of
each project.
Safety was also a significant focus during our discussion. Conjet emphasized that operator train­
ing is crucial for maintaining safety and project efficiency. The duration of this training ranges
from two days to a week, depending on the operator’s previous experience and the complexity of
the project. Conjet provides on­site training and follow­up support, ensuring that operators un­
derstand the risks associated with high­pressure water, which can range from 1500 to 3000 bar.
Proper setup, safe distance maintenance, and a thorough understanding of the machine are vital
for avoiding accidents. The hydrodemolition process also generates significant water and debris,
necessitating careful management to prevent contamination of the surrounding work area. Conjet
advised using tarps to seal the workspace, especially when removing material overhead, to mini­
mize environmental risks.
Hydrodemolition is a noisy process, though it has the advantage of minimal vibration compared to
traditional demolition methods, which can cause micro­cracks. Noise levels depend on the specific
environment but can be managed to remain within acceptable limits. Conjet shared an example
from a project in Japan where they successfully kept noise below 79 decibels.
In terms of operational productivity, Conjet’s robots are capable of removing between 0.15 to 0.25
cubic meters of concrete per hour. The efficiency is influenced by various factors, such as the
type of concrete, site access, and the ease with which the robot can move between sections. For
larger projects, using multiple robots can significantly expedite the process. Regular maintenance
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
92
==PAGE:102==
is essential for efficient operation, and Conjet recommended daily checks to minimize downtime
and keep the machines running optimally.
The cost of using hydrodemolition robots consists of multiple elements, including diesel for the
pump, electricity for the robot, water, wear parts, labor, and cleanup. Cleanup is particularly im­
portant but often overlooked. Each project is assessed individually to determine costs, which can
vary based on the type of concrete and reinforcement involved.
For our specific project, which involves dismantling elements in a residential building, Conjet
recommended using the Robot 101 NALTA. This model weighs only about 12 kg, significantly
lighter than their other models, which can weigh between 1 and 2.9 tons. Its lightweight design
makes it easier to move around in residential settings. Conjet also suggested that using the Robot
101 NALTA could be a cost­effective alternative to traditional methods like diamond sawing.
full interveiw with questions and answers can be found on next page
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark

==PAGE:103==
about the use case, We are investigating the feasibility using grout/mortar that is weaker 
than the concrete slabs so it would maybe be possible to remove only the mortar without 
damaging the other elements so the buildings could be taken apart and reused.
In general the specification of the concrete determines the force needed. The 
pressure (bar) is decided from the tensile strength of the concrete. Working range 
from 1500bar to 3000par.
General 
Has your product been used before with precast elements? If yes do you have a case study/project 
we could look into?
Are there any case studies or projects where hydrodemolition was used specifically in the context 
disassembly? If so, what were the results and key takeaways?
They will look into this and send us some case studies (project example)
Currently, Conjet’s hydrodemolition robots are used to remove concrete for repair purposes. In your 
expert opinion, do you think it would be feasible to adapt these robots to remove mortar between 
precast concrete elements and possibly screed? 
yes, it is being done and they sounded really positive that it would be possible to 
remove weak mortar between elements without damaging the elements with the 
Conjet meeting 10.17.2024 - Questions
   
Page 1
   
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
94
==PAGE:104==
correct specifications
Mortar removal between elements requires precision to avoid damaging the surrounding concrete. 
How precise are your robots in targeting specific materials, and would they be able to differentiate 
between mortar and adjacent concrete without causing damage to reusable elements?
Yes, it is possible to control the precision of the robots by adjusting both the 
pressure and the depth/width of the water jet. However, it’s important to consider that 
if there are pre-existing cracks in the concrete, the high-pressure water may infiltrate 
these cracks and cause additional breakage. While the process can be highly 
accurate under ideal conditions, it’s not guaranteed to achieve perfect precision every 
time, especially in compromised concrete. Therefore, caution must be exercised when 
aiming for high precision, particularly in scenarios where the surrounding material 
must remain intact. They suggest for this specific use case laying steel plates to the 
sides as the water will not go through steel. This could be a "template" so the cut will 
not go outside of specified range.
The robot can of course remove weaker material from a stronger material. f.x, a case 
in tunnel they removed spots of C20 concrete from C40 concrete. 
How much water flow is needed on site? Can the system be connected to regular water system, or is 
there a need to connect to a fire hydrant for example?
It totally depends on the project so really hard to say. But you will always need some 
sort of pump, to keep the robot going there needs to be x amount of water at all times. 
But the usage of water can be anywhere from min 20l per min, up to 220l per min. This 
much pressure does not work with the water system generally. The system can come with 
containers for the pump as seen on the conjet animation on youtube. Also note that the water 
must be collected and filtered on site. This is a seperate solution depending on location and 
access. Higher pressure equals more efficiency and time saving.
Safety
What safety protocols are in place when using Conjet’s hydrodemolition robots to remove concrete? 
How are operators and nearby workers protected from high-pressure water hazards, and what 
measures are in place to prevent accidental damage to other parts of the structure?
The primary safety measure when using Conjet’s hydrodemolition robots is to ensure 
that operators receive thorough training. The duration of the training depends on the 
complexity of the project and the prior experience of the operator, ranging from two 
days to a week. In some cases, Conjet’s own employees conduct on-site training, 
starting with indoor sessions, for certain projects, Conjet may follow the company on-
site during the initial use to provide hands-on support if necessary.
This training is essential for operators to understand how to handle high-pressure 
water safely and to prevent any hazards to nearby workers. Moreover, proper training 
helps operators avoid accidental damage to other parts of the structure, ensuring that 
only the targeted areas are removed during the hydrodemolition process.
The operator is key here. He will set up the system and close off safe distances if 
necessary. High pressure water is dangerous, 40bar will pierce through skin and the 
robots work with 1500 up to 3000 bar.
When the robot is in use, is there significant cleaning required afterward? For example, does the 
process leave a lot of water or debris, and are there any concerns about contamination of the 
surrounding area?
The short answer from them was YES! The hydrodemolition process involves high-
pressure water, which can generate significant amounts of water and debris 
   
Page 2
   
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
95
==PAGE:105==
depending on the scale of the operation. However, measures are typically in place to 
manage and contain both the water and debris. For example, water can be collected 
and filtered to prevent contamination of the surrounding area, and same goes for the 
debris which needs to be cleaned up afterwards. 
When removing concrete overhead using the hydrodemolition robot, where does the removed 
material go, and how is it managed to prevent it from falling or contaminating the surrounding area?
They did not have a specific answer to this question, the reality is that there will be a 
lot of water and debris and the methods for this is different for each case. Usually the 
company handling the robots on site have made some measures to ensure that the 
water can be collected and debris cleaned. That is not something Conjet specifically 
provides the companies. The rectangular steel frame and the rubber will guard the 
environment from most debris. Safest way is to seal the workplace with tarp and pay also close 
attention to what is below.
what are the noise and vibration levels generated by the hydrodemolition robots during concrete 
removal, How do you manage these factors to minimize disruption to the surrounding environment 
and ensure worker safety?
The noise levels are acknowledged as high, depending on the environment, but 
hydrodemolition offers the advantage of not introducing micro-cracks into the 
remaining structure. The speaker mentions that vibration is not a concern with 
hydrodemolition robots. They give an example from a site in Japan where noise levels 
were successfully kept below 79 decibels and, well within the acceptable range for

that project.
Time
On average, how long does it take for a Conjet hydrodemolition robot to remove concrete from a 
given section, for example, a slab or wall of a certain size? What factors most affect the time 
required, such as the type of concrete or reinforcement used?
The key determinant of productivity is the high-pressure operational hours, which 
depend on factors like access to the site and the size of the pump. The speakers 
mention that 0.15 to 0.25 cubic meters of concrete can be removed per high-pressure 
hour. Larger projects may take longer, but the robot's efficiency is influenced by 
logistical factors such as how quickly the robot can move between joints or sections. 
Check if this would change with weaker material
How larger are you projects typically? Such as are you removing concrete form several floors of a 
building or is it mainly small repairs? If the projects are large do multiple robots work 
simultaneously, and how is the overall timeline managed?
The speakers mention that project sizes vary widely, from large-scale jobs like airport 
landing strips to smaller bridge joint repairs. Regardless of size, the focus is on the 
precision and speed of the operation, especially for time-sensitive infrastructure 
projects like tunnels or bridges where downtime must be minimized. More robots can 
work at the same time in larger projects
How often do the robots require maintenance during an operation, and what impact does 
maintenance have on the overall project timeline? Are there any common issues that can lead to 
downtime?
The robots require daily checks and basic maintenance to ensure they 
continue operating efficiently. Basically the better care you take of the 
machine the less downtime you will have. But of course there is some 
maintenance and they recommended just going over the robot each day before 
starting. 
Cost
What are the typical costs associated with using Conjet’s hydrodemolition robots, either per square 
meter or cubic meter of concrete removed? How do these costs compare to traditional methods of 
concrete removal?
Cost factors include diesel for the pump, electricity for the robot, water, wear parts, 
labor, and cleanup. These are the key ingredients that contribute to the overall cost of 
using hydrodemolition robots. Clean-up is especially important, as it is often 
overlooked in the cost.
How much does the type of concrete or the amount of reinforcement affect the cost of 
hydrodemolition? If so, how does Conjet calculate and adjust costs for different types of concrete or 
structural conditions?
Each case is looked into

Hi Lea,
Sorry for the slow reply. Your project sounds very interesting!
In Gellerupparken, all hollow core slabs were dismantled using a diamond saw. In Danish, you
would call the type of saw a “wall saw.” It is mounted on “tracks” that are connected to the hollow
core slabs. This allows for more precise cutting. This type of method takes some time, so that is
a disadvantage. When you cut the hollow core slabs out, the saw also might remove part of the
hollow core slabs. This might be avoided if you are aware of it beforehand. But we didn’t think
about it, so the profiling on the side of the hollow core slabs was partly removed. The profiling in
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
102
==PAGE:112==
the bottom/lower part of the side removes the need for formwork when (re)casting the connection
between two hollow core slabs.
I’ve attached some pictures so it’s hopefully more clear what I mean ￿￿.
Best regards,
Katja Udbye Christensen

Hi Eiríkur Ingi,
For the lifting process, we did not recommend the solution shown in the picture. Mainly because
the contractor in our project (Per Aarsleff) doesn’t use this type of equipment. Their opinion is that
it’s too dangerous even for new constructions. We chose to follow their advice.
I’m not sure it was common to use holes for lifting precast concrete elements during installation in
Denmark. We have looked through a lot of old drawings and only seen this used one time. I think
mostly a type of anchor was used. However, we have been told that the manufacturer of lifting
equipment only guarantees a certain number of lifts. Therefore, we have recommended that you
install new anchors for lifting

Subject: Sv: Further info regarding reuse of hollow core slabs
Hi students,
Regarding your questions:
• Ombrukskartlegging iht. TEK17: Resource mapping of building materials, which is
obligatory before a project starts. TEK17 is the Norwegian building law, updated in 2017.
• Materialkatalog i Materia: Materia is our mapping system, where findings are catalogued.
See https://www.materia.no.
• Utarbeidet demonteringsbeskrivelse: Writing down how to dismantle: cutting between
the slabs, cutting on the top, etc.
• Gjennomført anbudsprosess: Description of the process and obtaining a price from a con­
structor/deconstructor.
• Documenting hollow core slab conditions: Measuring damages, documenting them, and
having an external engineer calculate the capacity and suggest repairs to restore the slabs.
Med vennlig hilsen,
Karen Izadi Nieuwenburg
Bærekraftsrådgiver ombruk

From: Thomas Fabian Delman (thomas@circue.dk)
To: Lea Jóhannesdóttir (s222673@student.dtu.dk)
Date: 14 January 2025, 09:43
Subject: Re: contact from WP, Questions regarding Circue’s platform and future plans
Hi Lea,
Thank you – hope you also enjoyed the holidays!
See my answers below.
Platform Accessibility: When do you plan to officially launch your platform? Will it be free
and open to everyone, or will access be restricted to certain companies or groups?
We plan to launch as a beta in a few months. In the beginning, we will use the platform in con­
junction with consulting services, both to make sure clients understand the value proposition, but
also to refine the use cases based on feedback from the first clients. A very basic part of the plat­
form will be free open access, e.g., basic estimates of embodied carbon and embedded resources
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
108
==PAGE:118==
as well as version 1 of the market exchange. In the long run, most services will probably be behind
a paywall (software­as­a­service), while others will be open access, and the business model will
involve transaction fees. Everyone who can see a benefit in using the services can use it.
User Adoption Strategy: Since documenting and uploading data takes both time and re­
sources, how do you plan to motivate companies and stakeholders to actively use your plat­
form? Are there incentives or programs in place to drive engagement?
Mostly, we believe that the value proposition lies in gaining more knowledge about your existing
building portfolio, the embodied carbon, embedded resources, and circular potentials. We provide
most of the data, as a user you mostly have to validate.
Data and Documentation: What strategies are being implemented to ensure the quality and
consistency of the data uploaded to the platform?
We’re guiding users and tracking every change made so we can always backtrack and see who
entered specific data.
Market Integration: Are you working with specific partners or industries to integrate your
platform into existing workflows, or is this something still in development?
We’re currently in dialogue with several market actors, from international software developers in
the building and construction industry to building owners, architects/engineering companies, con­
tractors, demolishers, etc. That has been a large part of our GS­projects.
Best,
­thomas
Thomas Fabian Delman

D
Case study
D.1
Wind calculations
The ρ factor is used to account for the lack of correlation of wind pressures between the windward
and leeward sides of the building, as described in the Eurocode. According to the provisions:
• For buildings with h/d ≥5, the resulting force is multiplied by 1.
• For buildings with h/d ≤1, the resulting force is multiplied by 0.85.
• For intermediate values of h/d, linear interpolation is applied.
• For the X­direction of the building, h/d = 0.64, which is less than 1. Therefore, ρ = 0.85
is used directly.
• For the Y­direction of the building, h/d = 2. Using linear interpolation, ρ is calculated as
follows:
ρ = 0.85 + (h/d −1)
(5 −1)
× (1.00 −0.85)
Substituting h/d = 2:
ρ = 0.85 + (2 −1)
4
× 0.15 = 0.85 + 0.0375 = 0.8875 (rounded to 0.89).
The internal pressure coefficient (Cpi) values for overpressure and underpressure are taken as 0.2
and −0.3, respectively, in accordance with standard assumptions for typical buildings. Using a
wind pressure (q) of 1.09 kN/m2, the resulting internal pressures are calculated as:
Wi,over = q · Cpi = 1.09 · 0.2 = 0.22 kN/m2
Wi,under = q · Cpi = 1.09 · (−0.3) = −0.33 kN/m2

D.4
Rigid floor methood
For walls aligned with the primary axes, the moments of inertia (Ix and Iy) are determined, and
the corresponding relative stiffness values (αx and αy) are used to allocate the loads.
The relative stiffness of each stabilizing wall is calculated based on its moment of inertia in both
the x­ and y­directions, derived using the wall’s thickness and length:
When analyzing a wall in the x­direction, the moments of inertia are defined as:
Iy = 1
12tL3,
Ix = 1
12Lt3
For a wall in the y­direction, the definitions are defined as:
Iy = 1
12Lt3,
Ix = 1
12tL3
Here, t is the wall thickness and L is its length.
In this case, Wall 1 in the x­direction is chosen as the reference wall, with its relative stiffness
set to αx1 = 1. The stiffness of the other walls is scaled relative to this reference wall using the
following formulas:
αxi = Iy,i
Iy,1
,
αyi = Ix,i
Iy,1
Here:
• Ix,i and Iy,i are the moments of inertia of Wall i in the x­ and y­directions, respectively.
• Iy,1 is the moment of inertia of the reference wall (Wall 1) in the y­direction.
This calculation ensures that the relative stiffness values (αxi and αyi) reflect the contributions of
each wall to the overall system stiffness. These relative stiffnesses are used in subsequent calcula­
tions, including determining the shear center and distributing the loads in the rigid floor method.
Figure D.1: Wall Geometry and Stiffness Properties of the Top Floor: Length, Thickness, Moment
of Inertia, and Relative Stiffness
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
129
==PAGE:139==
The shear center coordinates are determined based on the walls’ relative stiffness and their spatial
distribution. The coordinates xF and yF are calculated using:
xF =
"n
i=1 xiαxi
"n
i=1 αxi
,
yF =
"n
i=1 yiαyi
"n
i=1 αyi
where αxi and αyi are the relative stiffnesses in the x­ and y­directions, respectively.
Figure D.2 shows the calculations used to find the shear center coordinates, based on the relative
stiffness of each wall and their positions.
Figure D.2: Shear Center Calculations for the Top Floor
The shear center is located centrally in the x­direction, between the walls spanning that direction.
However, in the y­direction, it is offset due to the distribution of relative stiffness and wall place­
ment. This is illustrated in Figure D.3, which shows the position of the shear center in the floor
plan of the building.
Figure D.3: Roof Wall Plan Showing the Location of the Shear Center
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
130
==PAGE:140==
The torsional stiffness (Iz) is calculated first because it’s needed to distribute the horizontal loads
and torsional effects to the walls. It’s found using this formula:
Iz =
n
!
i=1
αxi(yi −yF )2 + αyi(xi −xF )2
Here, αxi and αyi are the relative stiffnesses, and (xF , yF ) are the shear center coordinates.
Figure D.4: Relative Torsional Stiffness Calculations for the Top Floor Walls
The critical load case is calculated by using the external wind load per square meter (We) and
multiplying it by the influential zone height (H = 1.5 m) to obtain the linear load (F). The linear
load is then multiplied by the floor length (L) or width (B) to find the equivalent point load (Pd).
For the x direction:
Pd = We · H · B = 1.53 · 1.5 · 13.7 = 51.77 kN.
For the y direction:
Pd = We · H · L = 1.58 · 1.5 · 22.4 = 87.79 kN.
To calculate the reaction forces distributed to each wall, the following formulas are used:
Rxi = αxi
# Wx
" αxi
−(yi −yF )MW
Iz
$
Ryi = αyi
# Wy
" αyi
+ (xi −xF )MW
Iz
$
Here, αxi and αyi represent the relative stiffnesses of each wall, Wx and Wy are the horizontal
loads, MW is the moment about the shear center, and Iz is the torsional stiffness.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
131
==PAGE:141==
The results of the reaction forces are shown in the following figures:
Figure D.5: Reaction Forces in the Walls Due to Wind Load from the +x Direction
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
132
==PAGE:142==
Figure D.6: Reaction Forces in the Walls Due to Wind Load from the +y Direction
The reaction force tables demonstrate how the wind load is distributed among the walls. For the
+x­direction, the total reaction forces from the walls sum to 51.77 kN, while for the +y­direction,
they sum to 87.79 kN. These values match the calculated point loads applied to the system, con­
firming equilibrium and the correctness of the method. This consistency validates the distribution
of loads and the application of the rigid floor method.
D.5
Autodesk Robot Structural Analysis
Top floor Robot results
A FEM model is used in this analysis to better understand the structural behavior of the top floor. By
using FEM, the interaction between the walls and the floor diaphragm is captured more accurately,
accounting for variations in stiffness and load distribution that simplified methods might not fully
address.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
133
==PAGE:143==
Figure D.7: Robot Model of the Top Floor – Wall Orientation and Diaphragm
Each floor was modeled as a separate Robot Structural Analysis model, containing key structural
elements such as walls and slabs. The walls are oriented in both the x­ and y­directions and are fixed
at the bottom to provide stability, while the slab acts as a rigid diaphragm to distribute horizontal
loads to the walls.
The only loads applied to the model are horizontal wind loads. Self­weight or dead loads are
excluded to maintain consistency with the rigid floor hand calculation method.
The 2D floor plan shows the layout of the walls, with walls in the y­direction shown in green and
walls in the x­direction shown in blue. This layout is used as the basis for applying wind loads and
analyzing the reaction forces.
The walls have a thickness of 200 mm. This setup ensures structural consistency and simplifies
the analysis.
Figure D.8: Application of wind load in the x­direction as a line load on the gable walls.
The horizontal wind load is applied to the structure as a uniform line load acting along the walls.
For the x­direction, the wind load is applied along the gable walls, representing wind blowing
perpendicular to the long side of the building. The applied line load is shown in Figure D.8.
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark
134
==PAGE:144==
Figure D.9: Application of wind load in the y­direction as a line load on the facade walls
Similarly, for the y­direction, the wind load is applied along the facade walls, representing wind
blowing perpendicular to the short side of the building. The applied line load is shown in Figure
D.9.
The reaction forces in the walls due to the wind load applied in the y­direction (θ = 0) are shown
in Figure D.10. The largest forces are observed in walls 1 and 3, as these walls have greater length,
resulting in higher moments of inertia and stiffness. These walls act as the primary load­resisting
elements in the y­direction, effectively transferring the horizontal wind load to the foundation.
Figure D.10: Roof plan: Reaction forces from Robot of walls in Y­direction.
Similarly, the reaction forces due to the wind load applied in the x­direction (θ = 90) are shown in
Figure D.11. In this case, walls 1 and 7 resist the majority of the load, as they are oriented along
the x­direction and are strategically placed to bear the wind­induced forces. Their stiffness and
alignment make them the primary contributors to the load transfer in this direction.

D.6
Vertical load calculations
These calculations determine the forces exerted by the slabs on the walls, taking into account live
loads, the length of the slabs, the thickness of the walls supporting the slabs, and whether the slabs
act on one side or both sides of the wall. The calculations also consider the minimum and maximum
normal forces, as well as the surface and line loads, derived from the given slab dimensions.

• Poisson’s ratio ν ­ DS/EN 1992­1­1, Section 3.1.3(4)
Poisson’s ratio for uncracked concrete is generally taken as 0.2 for design purposes.
• Shear modulus G ­ DS/EN 1992­1­1, Section 3.1.3
Calculated using the relationship between the modulus of elasticity, E and Poisson’s ratio,
ν.
G =
E
2(1 + ν
(E.1)
• Young’s Modulus E ­ DS/EN 1992­1­1, Section 3.1.3 (3)
Values of modulus of elasticity for different concrete classes are provided in table 3.1.
• Yield strength ­ Eurocode does not explicitly define a yield strength for concrete but relates
compressive strength to various stress parameters. So the yield strength is assumed to be
10% of the compressive strength (fck).
Y ieldStrength = 0.1 · fck
(E.2)
• Compressive strength fck ­ DS/EN 1992­1­1, Section 3.1.2 and table 3.1 provides values
for concrete various concrete classes.

Deklarationens ejer 
Dansk Beton 
Fabriksbetonforeningen 
CVR: 16983098 
 
Udgivet af 
EPD Danmark 
www.epddanmark.dk 
 
☒ Branche EPD   
☐ Produkt EPD 
Deklareret produkt 
1m3 fabriksbeton til anvendelse i eksponeringsklasse XC2, XC3, 
XC4, XF1 og XA1. Dette svarer til beton udsat for moderat 
miljøpåvirkning som defineret i DS/EN 206 DK NA.  
 
EPD’en er udarbejdet på baggrund af vægtede gennemsnitsdata 
fra 
flere 
producenter 
(average 
product, 
Industry 
level). 
Producenterne som leverer data til EPD’en dækker ca. 80% af 
den samlede danske produktion af fabriksbeton. 
Markedsandelen er gældende for den totale produktion af 
fabriksbeton, og ikke hver enkelt produktvariant. 
 
Antal deklarerede datasæt/produktvariationer: 2  
C30/37 (sætmålsbeton) 
C35/45 SCC (selvkompakterende beton) 
Produktionssted 
Danske betonproducenter, der har leveret til branche-EPD’en; 
IBF, Unicon, og DK Beton 
Produktets anvendelse 
Fabriksbeton i eksponeringsklasserne XC2, XC3, XC4, XF1 og 
XA1 anvendes til:  Beton udsat for langvarig kontakt med vand 
(XC2), beton indendørs med moderat eller høj luftfugtighed eller 
beton udendørs beskyttet mod regn (XC3), beton udsat for 
kontakt med vand, herunder fx udvendige vægge, facader, 
altaner mv. (XC4), vertikale betonoverflader udsat for regn og 
frost (XF1), og beton udsat for jord og grundvand med let 
aggressiv kemisk miljø (XA1). Informative eksempler kan ses i 
DS/EN 206 DK NA. 
Deklareret/funktionel enhed 
Den deklarerede enhed er 1 m3 fabriksbeton 
Årstal for data 
2018 
Udstedt 
15-03-2021 
Gyldig til: 
20-07-2025 
Beregningsgrundlag 
Denne miljøvaredeklaration er udviklet 
iht. til 
kravene i EN 15804+A1.  
 
Sammenlignelighed 
Miljøvaredeklarationer for byggevarer er muligvis 
ikke sammenlignelige hvis ikke de overholder 
kravene i EN 15804. EPD data er muligvis ikke 
sammenlignelig med mindre alle anvendte datasæt 
er 
udviklet 
i 
henhold 
til 
EN 
15804 
og 
baggrundssystemerne baseres på samme database. 
 
Gyldighed 
Denne miljøvaredeklaration er verificeret i henhold 
til kravene i ISO 14025 og er gyldig i 5 år fra 
udstedelsesdatoen 
 
Anvendelse 
Den tilsigtede anvendelse af miljøvaredeklarationen 
er, 
at 
kommunikere 
videnskabeligt 
baserede 
miljøinformationer for produktet til/fra professionelle 
aktører 
med 
det 
formål, 
at 
kunne 
vurdere 
miljøpåvirkninger for bygninger.

nedenfor. Disse udgør 100 vægt % af det

Repræsentativitet 
Den deklarerede enhed er 1 m3 fabriksbeton 
baseret på en repræsentativ markedsandel for 
fabriksbeton solgt af danske producenter til den 
danske byggebranche. 
 
Ved hjælp af fagspecialister er det vurderet at 
de udvalgte produktionssteder er repræsentative 
for produktionsmetoder og blandingsforhold for 
den totale nationale produktion af fabriksbeton. 
Produktionsstederne 
er 
valgt 
med 
jævn 
geografisk spredning. 
 
Data til den bagvedliggende LCA er baseret på 
årsgennemsnit for produktionen af udvalgte 
typer af fabriksbeton hos producenterne for 
2018. 
 
Baggrundsdata er baseret på GaBi databasen 
2019, samt produktspecifikke EPD’er. Disse data 
er for de fleste <5 år gamle, og alle datasæt er 
<10 
år 
gamle 
i 
overensstemmelse 
med 
EN15804:2012+A1:2013. 
 
Indhold af farlige stoffer 
Produktet indeholder ikke stoffer fra REACH Kan-
didatlisten, ”Candidate List of Substances of 
Very High Concern for authorisation”, hvis 
indhold 
overskrider 
0,1 
vægt 
% 
(http://echa.europa.eu/candidate-list-table).  
 
 
Væsentlige egenskaber 
Fabriksbeton 
skal 
efterleve 
kravene 
i 
betonstandarden 
DS/EN-206 
med 
nationalt 
tillægsanneks: 
DS/EN 
206 
NA 
DK, 
samt 
prøvningsstandarder i serierne DS/EN 12350 og 
DS/EN 12390. 
 
 
 
Levetid (RSL) 
Levetiden regnes som 100 år (RSL) jf. Annex AA 
i ”DS/EN 16757:2017 – ”Bæredygtighed inden 
for byggeri og anlæg – miljøvaredeklarationer – 
Produktkategoriregler for beton og betonele-
menter”.

for

hvori

MD-20012-DA_rev1 | Dansk Beton Fabriksbetonforeningen | Side 5 af 12 
Systemgrænse 
EPD’en er baseret på en vugge-til-grav LCA, 
hvor alle relevante og afgørende processer fra 
livscyklussen 
er 
medregnet 
dog 
undtaget 
indbygning/installation (A5).  
 
Brugsfaserne (B2-B7) er vurderet til ikke at have 
relevans for EPD’en, da der ikke forekommer 
bidrag så længe produktet er installeret i en 
given 
bygning/konstruktion 
i 
henhold 
til 
gældende anvisninger og standarder.  
 
De generelle regler for udeladelse af inputs og 
outputs i LCA’en følger bestemmelserne i EN 
15804:2012+A1:2013, 6.3.5, hvor den totale 
udeladelse af input flow pr. modul højst må 
være 5 % af energiforbrug og masse, og max 
1% per enhedsproces. 
 
Nøgleantagelser for systemgrænsen er beskrev-
et for hvert livscyklusstadie nedenfor.  
 
Produktfasen (A1-A3): 
Produktfasen omfatter tilvejebringelsen af alle 
råmaterialer, produkter og energi, transport til 
produktionen, blandingsproces, intern transport 
samt affaldsbehandling frem til ”end-of-waste” 
eller endelig bortskaffelse. LCA-resultaterne er 
angivet i aggregeret form for produktfasen, 
hvilket betyder, at modulerne A1, A2 og A3 
betragtes som et samlet modul A1-A3. 
 
Fabriksbeton 
fremkommer 
ved 
en 
produktionsmetode, hvor alle delmaterialer blan-
des sammen iht. relevante standarder typisk i 
en tvangsblander. Fabriksbetonen distribueres 
direkte fra produktion til byggepladsen vha. 
betonkanon (rotérbil). 
 
Energiproduktion ved forbrænding af affald fra 
A3, 
enten 
ved 
intern 
forbrænding 
eller 
forbrændingsanlæg, 
er 
allokeret 
indenfor 
systemgrænsen, og bidragene er modregnet 
forbrug af varme og el. 
 
Byggeprocesfasen (A4): 
Byggeprocesfasen 
omfatter 
transport 
fra 
fabriksporten til byggepladsen (med beton-
kanon/rotérbil). 
 
Armering og andre sekundære materialer, der 
installeres 
ifm. 
brug 
af 
fabriksbeton 
på 
byggepladsen, er ikke inkluderet i nærværende 
EPD, og skal derfor tillægges i fald de anvendes. 
 
Brugsfasen (B1-B7): 
Når produktet først er installeret i bygningen vil 
der under normale brugsforhold ikke være behov 
for vedligehold, reparationer, udskiftninger eller 
renovering. Ligeledes er der heller ikke hverken 
energi- 
eller 
vandforbrug 
forbundet 
med 
produktet i brugsfasen. Optag af CO2, som følge 
af karbonatisering i produktet, er medtaget i 
LCA’en og deklareret i modul B1. 
 
Omfanget af karbonatisering er afhængigt af 
brugsscenariet for fabriksbetonen. Der er derfor 
angivet to forskellige værdier for karbonatisering 
repræsenterende hhv. forhold med lav grad af 
karbonatisering og forhold med høj grad af 
karbonatisering. 
For 
forhold 
med 
lav 
karbonatisering er der regnet på anvendelsen i 
hhv. 
bundplader 
(500mm) 
eller 
dæk/gulve 
(200mm) og for høj karbonatisering er der 
regnet på anvendelsen som indvendig væg 
(tykkelse 200mm).  
 
 
Endt levetid (C1-C4): 
Ved endt levetid af betonkonstruktioner, vil de 
oftest 
blive 
revet 
ned 
vha. 
gravemaskine 
monteret med betonhammer eller betonsaks. 
Herefter læsses betonen i container/lastbil med 
gravemaskine.  
 
Den 
nedbrudte 
beton 
transporteres 
fra 
nedrivningsplads til affaldsbehandler med lastbil. 
 
Endt levetid omfatter nedrivning, indledende on-
site sortering/knusning, transport til behand-
lingssted samt deponi, affaldsbehandling og 
bortskaffelse af ikke-genanvendeligt materiale. 
Materialeandelen der genanvendes nedknuses 
inden den anvendes i næste produktsystem. I 
Danmark genanvendes >90% af betonaffald, 
hvoraf størstedelen udlægges som stabiliserende 
bærelag under veje, i denne EPD regnes et 
scenarie med 97% genanvendelse af betonen, 
og 3% til deponi. 
 
Nedknust beton afsættes til genanvendelse som 
ubundet bærelag i opbygning af nye veje og 
pladser. Den nedknuste beton indgår i følgende 
produkter: 
1. Rent knust beton 
2. Genbrugsstabil (en blanding mellem knust 
beton og asfalt) 
3. Genbrugsballast (en blanding mellem knust 
beton og knust tegl), herunder falder også 
den fine fraktion af nedknust beton. 
 
De forskellige produkter læsses på lastbil og 
transporteres til modtagelokaliteten 
 
Potentiale for genbrug, genanvendelse og 
energigenvinding (D): 
Ved anvendelse af knust beton i forbindelse med 
opbygning af veje og pladser vil betonen oftest 
erstatte anvendelsen af stabilgrus fra grusgrav. 
Genanvendelsen 
af 
knust 
beton 
reducerer 
derved forbruget af stabilgrus. 
Design of dismantling methods of precast concrete element buildings in Denmark

LCA resultater 
Til beregning af LCIA resultater er karakteriseringsmodellen CML 2001 anvendt sammen med GaBi 8.7 
databasen til klassificering og karakterisering af input- og output flows. Dette er i henhold til EN 15804 
6.5 samt Annex C.  
Livscyklusfaserne A4-D er baseret på de samme processer og scenarier, men da densiteten varierer 
mellem de enkelte produkttyper, varierer resultaterne også.

Indeluft 
EPD’en angiver ikke noget omkring afgivelse af 
farlige stoffer til indeluften, da de horisontale 
standarder for måling af afgivelse af regulerede 
farlige stoffer fra byggevarer ved brug af 
harmoniserede 
test 
metoder 
i 
henhold 
til 
bestemmelserne fra de respektive tekniske 
komitéer for Europæiske produktstandarder ikke 
er tilgængelige. 
 
Jord og vand 
EPD’en angiver ikke noget omkring afgivelse af 
farlige stoffer til jord og vand, da de horisontale 
standarder for måling af afgivelse af regulerede 
farlige stoffer fra byggevarer ved brug af 
harmoniserede 
test 
metoder 
i 
henhold 
til 
bestemmelserne fra de respektive tekniske 
komitéer for Europæiske produktstandarder ikke 
er tilgængelige.

Betonelement-Foreningen

Udgivet af 
EPD Danmark

☒ Branche EPD   
☐ Produkt EPD 
Deklareret produkt 
1 m2 vægelement, med 11% udsparinger. 
 
EPD’en er udarbejdet på baggrund af vægtede gennemsnitsdata 
fra flere producenter (average product, Industry level). 
Producenterne som leverer data til EPD’en dækker ca. 84% af 
den samlede danske produktion af vægelementer. 
 
Antal deklarerede datasæt/produktvariationer: 3 
15 cm tyk væg, 11% udsparinger, med 5-15 kg armering 
20 cm tyk væg, 11% udsparinger,  med 5-15 kg armering 
20 cm tyk væg, 11% udsparinger,  med 16-25 kg armering 
Produktionssted 
Danske betonproducenter, der har leveret data til branche 
EPD’en; CRH Concrete A/S, Spæncom A/S, Contiga A/S, 
Ambercon A/S, Industri Beton A/S, Give Elementfabrik A/S, 
Confac A/S, Thisted-Fjerrritslev Cementvarefabrik, Dan-element 
A/S, 
Gandrup 
Element 
A/S, 
Midtjydsk 
Beton-vare 
og 
Elementfabrik A/S, & Leth Beton A/S. 
Produktets anvendelse 
Betonelement til byggeri. 
Deklareret/funktionel enhed 
Deklareret enhed er 1 m2 vægelement, med 11% udsparinger. 
Årstal for data 
2018 
 
Udstedt 
15-03-2021 
Gyldig til: 
20-07-2025 
Beregningsgrundlag 
Denne miljøvaredeklaration er udviklet iht. til kravene 
i EN 15804+A1.  
 
Sammenlignelighed 
Miljøvaredeklarationer for byggevarer er muligvis 
ikke sammenlignelige hvis ikke de overholder 
kravene i EN 15804. EPD data er muligvis ikke 
sammenlignelig med mindre alle anvendte datasæt er 
udviklet 
i 
henhold 
til 
EN 
15804 
og 
baggrundssystemerne baseres på samme database. 
 
Gyldighed 
Denne miljøvaredeklaration er verificeret i henhold til 
kravene i ISO 14025 og er gyldig i 5 år fra 
udstedelsesdatoen 
 
Anvendelse 
Den tilsigtede anvendelse af miljøvaredeklarationen 
er, 
at 
kommunikere 
videnskabeligt 
baserede 
miljøinformationer for produktet til/fra professionelle 
aktører 
med 
det 
formål, 
at 
kunne 
vurdere 
miljøpåvirkninger for bygninger.  
 
EPD type

☒Vugge-til-port med tilvalg 
☐Vugge-til-grav

Repræsentativitet 
Den deklarerede enhed er 1 m2 vægelement med 
11% udsparinger, med en betondensitet op til 
2350 kg/m3, baseret på en repræsentativ 
markedsandel for vægelementer solgt af danske 
producenter. 
Ved hjælp af fagspecialister er det vurderet, at de 
udvalgte produktionssteder er repræsentative for 
produktionsmetoder og sammensætninger for 
den totale nationale produktion af vægelementer. 
Produktionsstederne 
er 
valgt 
med 
jævn 
geografisk spredning. 
Data til den bagvedliggende LCA er baseret på 
årsgennemsnit for produktionen af udvalgte typer 
af betonelementer hos producenterne for 2018. 
Baggrundsdata er baseret på GaBi databasen 
2019, samt produktspecifikke EPD’er. Disse data 
er for de fleste <5 år gamle, og alle datasæt er 
<10 
år 
gamle 
i 
overensstemmelse 
med 
EN15804:2012+A1:2013. 
 
Indhold af farlige stoffer 
Produktet indeholder ikke stoffer fra REACH Kan-
didatlisten, ”Candidate List of Substances of 
Very High Concern for authorisation”, hvis 
indhold overskrider 0,1 vægt % 
(http://echa.europa.eu/candidate-list-table).  
Væsentlige egenskaber (CE) 
Betonelementer skal efterleve kravene i 
produktstandarderne. For vægge DS/EN 14992. 
Der er udformet ydeevnedeklarationer af de 
enkelte produkter hos den enkelte producent. Til 
branche EPD’erne er der anvendte data fra tolv 
repræsentative 
producenter. 
Ydeevnedeklara-
tioner kan erhverves direkte hos producenterne 
ved forespørgsel. 
Levetid 
(RSL) 
Levetiden regnes som 100 år (RSL) jf. Annex AA 
i ”DS/EN 16757:2017 – ”Bæredygtighed inden for 
byggeri og anlæg – miljøvaredeklarationer – 
Produktkategoriregler for beton og betonele-
menter”.

Funktionel enhed 
Ikke defineret. 
Betonelementer leveres færdige, hvor ekstra 
tilsætningsmaterialer, herunder fugebeton

anvendes.
